If dysgraphia is the main issue, spelling drills done only on paper can backfire, because the child’s working memory and attention are being drained by letter formation. You may be trying to build orthographic mapping, but the child is spending their attention budget on grip, pressure, and staying on the line. The practice becomes a test of

endurance, not a clean mapping opportunity.

This matters because it changes what “more practice” means.

In Chapter 1 we talked about the mental sticky note that lasts long enough to pass Friday’s test and then disappears by Monday. Dyslexia often keeps a child stuck in sticky-note learning because mapping does not happen automatically. The solution is structured mapping: fewer words, explicit sound work, deliberate sound-to-letter connections, and repetition with attention.

Dysgraphia can create a different sticky-note problem: the child might have the mapping, but the act of handwriting knocks the sticky note off mid-task. Their brain is trying to hold the sounds of the word while the hand demands constant supervision. The solution is to reduce output load while you strengthen output skills, so the child can actually use what they know.

That brings us to the most important practical difference for families: accommodations are not the same for dyslexia and dysgraphia, even though some overlap.

For dyslexia, the central need is instruction that builds decoding and encoding explicitly, the kind of multisensory structured literacy approach we will walk through later in this book. Tools like audiobooks, text-to- speech, and spell-check can support access to content, but they do not replace the need to build the sound-to-print pathway if reading and spelling are the goals.

For dysgraphia, the central need is often a different output channel while handwriting develops, and sometimes even long-term. Typing, speech-to- text dictation, a scribe for longer assignments, reduced copying, and alternatives to handwritten tests can be the difference between a child showing their knowledge and a child being graded on the bottleneck. This is why Chapter 9 will matter so much. Tools are not crutches when they are matched to the disability. They are ramps.

You can see how this lowers the temperature we’ve been talking about since Chapter 1.

When dysgraphia is present, a parent can unknowingly turn every homework session into a double demand: “Hold the spelling in your mind and also make your hand perform perfectly.” The child melts down, and the adult concludes the child is being resistant. The child concludes they are failing at something that feels basic. Shame grows.

But if you name the difference, you can say something far more accurate: “Your ideas are fine. Your spelling practice is one thing. Your handwriting is another thing. We’re going to handle them separately so your brain isn’t doing two hard jobs at once.”

That sentence can change a child’s whole posture at the table.

It also helps you interpret effort correctly. A dyslexic child may look like they’re working hard because they are: they’re doing heavy language processing to decode and encode. A dysgraphic child may look like they’re working hard because they are: they’re doing heavy motor planning and visual-motor coordination to form letters and keep them on the page. Both kinds of effort are real. Both deserve respect. But they require different kinds of support.

So how do you tell which one is leading the dance in your child?

You don’t need a perfect diagnosis to start observing patterns.

Notice what happens when the output channel changes. If spelling improves dramatically when your child types, uses letter tiles, or dictates, that points toward dysgraphia being a major factor. If spelling remains highly inconsistent across handwriting, typing, and oral spelling, dyslexia may be the stronger driver. If both are true, then you may be looking at the common overlap: a child who struggles to map words and also struggles to get them out through the hand.

Notice also what costs your child the most. Some children lose the spelling at the sound level. Others lose it at the letter formation level. Others lose it when they have to do both plus compose a sentence.

And remember the relay race model from Chapter 2. Dyslexia tends to disrupt the handoff between sounds and letters. Dysgraphia tends to disrupt the handoff between letters and the hand. If you can locate the drop, you can stop yelling at the runner and start training the handoff.

The point of separating dysgraphia from dyslexia is not to slap a second label on your child. It is to stop treating every writing struggle as if it has one cause and one cure. It is to give you permission to adjust the task, protect your child’s confidence, and choose tools that match the real bottleneck.

Because when the hand won’t cooperate, the most compassionate and effective response is not “Try harder.” It is “Let’s make the path easier for your hand while we teach your brain and body the steps, slowly, steadily, without shame.”

In the next section, we’re going to zoom in on that motor component more closely, because recognizing it is one thing. Knowing what it looks like in real time, and why it steals spelling and compositional ability so quickly, is another. Once you can see the motor load clearly, you can start making small changes that produce fast relief, even before you have a full plan in place.

There is a particular moment that can help you spot dysgraphia in real time, and it has almost nothing to do with spelling rules.

It is the moment your child knows what they want to say, maybe even knows how to spell the key word, and yet their whole body changes when you hand them the pencil.

Their shoulders creep up. Their grip tightens. Their breathing gets shallow. They stall. They sharpen the pencil again. They erase a perfectly fine letter and try to rewrite it. They ask you how to make a capital G, even though you have shown them a hundred times. Or they start writing and you can see the effort spreading across their face as if they are lifting something heavy.

That is the motor component. And recognizing it is one of the fastest ways to lower the temperature at the table, because it lets you stop misreading a physical bottleneck as a character problem.

Motor is not just “hand strength.” It is the whole chain of planning and executing movement: where the pencil starts, which direction the stroke goes, how much pressure to use, how to stop at the right point, how to space letters, how to keep the writing on the line, how to shift from one letter to the next without losing your place. For a child with dysgraphia, that chain is not automatic. Each link demands attention.

If you want to see how big this is, try a small experiment. Ask your child to tell you a sentence out loud about something they love. Many children who struggle with handwriting will give you a sentence that is complex, funny, and full of detail.

Now ask them to write the same sentence.

If the sentence shrinks from “The volcano exploded because the pressure built up under the mountain and the lava went everywhere” to “Volcano big,” you have learned something important. The thinking did not disappear. The output channel narrowed what could fit through it.

This is why dysgraphia can quietly rewrite your child’s school identity.

Teachers see the written product. The written product is small, messy, incomplete, or filled with errors. It is easy, even for kind adults, to assume the child doesn’t know much, or isn’t trying, or is rushing. Meanwhile, the child is doing a complicated juggling act: holding the idea, holding the sentence, holding the spelling, and also managing a hand that needs constant supervision.

In Chapter 2 we used the relay race model. Dyslexia often disrupts the handoff between sounds and letters. Dysgraphia often disrupts the handoff between letters and the hand. This section is about noticing, with clarity and without blame, when the handoff to the hand is where things fall apart.

One clue is the difference between oral spelling and written spelling.

Some dysgraphic children can spell a word out loud correctly, in order, and then write something different. They are not lying. They are not being silly. The act of forming the letters can knock the word out of working memory.

Remember that spelling is retrieval under load. Handwriting adds load. If your child has to think hard about how to form the letter b, they may lose the next sound in the word. If they pause to remember whether g goes below the line, the beginning of the word may fade. If their grip is tight and their hand is tired, their brain may rush to escape the discomfort, and letters get dropped.

Another clue is what happens mid-word.

Watch for the stall in the middle of a word your child knows. The pencil stops. The child’s eyes go up and away, not in a daydreaming way, but in a searching way. Sometimes they whisper the word again. Sometimes they shake their hand. Sometimes they start over. This pause often looks like a spelling problem. But very often, it is the motor plan for the next letter failing to appear quickly enough, which interrupts the spelling sequence.

A child who is mapping a word but is slowed by handwriting is like a person trying to say a sentence while someone pinches their arm every few seconds. They can still speak, but the interruption changes the whole flow. That is what a difficult letter can be for a dysgraphic child: an interruption strong enough to derail the rest of the word.

You may also see a specific pattern: the first few letters are clearer, then the writing degrades.

This is not always “giving up.” It can be fatigue. It can be the way attention drains when every letter requires conscious control. Some children can produce one neat line, especially if they are fresh or trying to impress. By the third line, the letters spread, tilt, compress, or collapse. They may press harder. They may start to curl around the page. This is the motor system losing efficiency under sustained demand.

If your child complains of hand pain, take it seriously.

Handwriting should not regularly hurt. Many children will say “my hand hurts” because they dislike the task, but dysgraphic children often mean it literally. Tight grip, high pressure, and slow, effortful strokes can create real discomfort. Pain creates urgency, and urgency increases errors. It also pushes the nervous system toward threat mode, which we have already named as the enemy of retrieval. A child who is writing through discomfort is not in a good state to map words or to show what they know.

Another clue is how your child behaves around copying.

In Chapter 1 we talked about copying as practice that can be oddly empty for spelling, because it allows recognition rather than retrieval. For a dysgraphic child, copying is also physically and visually demanding. It requires constant eye shifts from the model to the paper and back again, and it requires holding a small chunk of visual information in working memory long enough to reproduce it. If working memory is already strained by motor control, copying becomes a perfect storm.

That is why some children copy words incorrectly even when the word is right in front of them. They are not being careless. The system cannot hold the visual chunk steady while also executing the motor plan.

You may see skipped letters, repeated letters, or words that begin accurately and then drift. You may see your child lose their place on the page, copy the same line twice, or omit whole words. When this is happening, adding more copying is like adding more weight to an overloaded bridge. It does not strengthen it. It just increases collapse.

Another clue is the mismatch between different tools.

If your child’s writing improves dramatically when they type, that does not automatically mean dysgraphia, but it is a strong hint. Typing reduces some motor demands: the letters are pre-formed, and the movement is different. Many dysgraphic kids can produce longer, clearer work on a keyboard because they are no longer spending most of their attention budget on forming letters and controlling a pencil.

Similarly, if your child can spell better with letter tiles, a magnetic board, or writing in sand, that can be a clue. Those options slow the process down and reduce fine motor strain. They also make it easier to correct without erasing and rewriting, which lowers the emotional load.

Notice what happens when you remove the handwriting demand. Does the spelling become steadier? Does the sentence become longer? Does your child’s personality come back into the work? Those are not small observations. They are diagnostic clues you can use even before any formal evaluation.

Now, a word about letter reversals, because they live right on the border between dyslexia and dysgraphia.

Many children reverse letters when they are young. That alone does not tell you much. But if reversals persist, and especially if they appear in handwriting more than in other formats, pay attention to the possibility that the motor recipe for the letter is not stable.

Some dysgraphic children know the difference between b and d intellectually but cannot consistently execute the correct formation under speed or pressure. The letter does not feel automatic. It has to be built each time. Under fatigue, the hand defaults to the easier or more familiar movement, and the reversal appears.

This is different from a child who reverses because the sound-letter mapping is fuzzy or because they have not anchored the word form. On the page, both can look the same. In your child’s body, they are not the same.

You can often tell by looking at the child’s effort.

A dysgraphic child may stare at the letter, hesitate, form it slowly, then still get it wrong. They may redo it several times. They may say, “I can’t remember which way it goes,” not because they have never been taught, but because the motor plan does not stick. Their hand does not have a reliable script.

This brings us to one of the most important mindset shifts in this chapter: handwriting is a skill, not a virtue.

Neat, fast handwriting is often treated like a sign of maturity or responsibility. Kids absorb that message early. If they cannot produce it, they may conclude they are sloppy, lazy, babyish, or broken. But handwriting is a complex motor-linguistic skill. Some children automate it

easily. Some children do not. That difference is not a moral failing.

When you recognize the motor component, you can change the conversation at home immediately.

Instead of “You’re not being careful,” you can say, “Your hand is working really hard.”

Instead of “Fix your handwriting,” you can say, “Let’s make this easier on your hand so your brain can focus on the words.”

Instead of “Just try,” you can say, “We’re going to separate the jobs. First we’ll get the idea down. Then we’ll worry about handwriting.”

That last sentence is powerful because it respects the reality of limited working memory. If your child is trying to generate ideas, spell, and form letters all at once, they are doing three hard things simultaneously. Many adults cannot do that without slowing down. A dysgraphic child is being asked to do it with a motor system that does not automate well. Separation is not lowering standards. It is creating access.

You can also start noticing when your child’s writing breakdown is really a signal of overload, not defiance.

If the meltdown happens after a certain amount of handwriting, if the writing gets worse as the assignment goes on, if the child’s body shows strain, if the child produces far more when allowed to dictate, those are patterns. They are the nervous system telling you, “We are out of capacity.”

That is information you can use.

It tells you when to shorten the written output, when to offer a break, when to let the child type, when to let them dictate, and when to stop turning writing into the place where confidence goes to die.

We are not yet in the chapter about tools, but it helps to say it here: recognizing the motor component is what gives you permission to use supports without guilt. If the bottleneck is motor, then insisting on handwritten output for everything is not “building character.” It is grading a disability. It is also draining the exact energy your child needs to build spelling and writing skills over time.

So hold this as you move forward: when the hand won’t cooperate, your job is not to increase pressure until it does. Your job is to notice the motor load, lower the total demand, and practice the right subskills in a calmer

lane.

In the next chapter we will lower the temperature emotionally, because writing struggles and spelling struggles almost always come with shame attached. But even before that, you can begin lowering it simply by naming what you see, accurately.

“This is the hard part for your hand.”

“That was a lot of writing. Your hand is tired.”

“Let’s take the motor part out for a minute so we can show what you know.”

For a child who has been told, directly or indirectly, that their messy page is proof of who they are, those sentences land like water on a fire. They do not solve everything. But they shift the story from “I’m bad” to “This is a hard channel.” And once the story shifts, the child has room to learn.

Chapter 4·First, Lower the Temperature —

Ending the Shame and Tears

By the time families reach for a book like this, they are rarely only asking a spelling question.

They are asking an emotional question.

Why does this small thing, a handful of words, keep turning into a fight? Why does my child look wounded by something that seems so ordinary? Why do I feel my own body tense up when the teacher sends home the weekly list? Why does a ten-minute assignment end with tears, or anger, or that blank, shut-down stare that means, “I can’t do one more thing”?

If you have been living in the Friday-to-Monday loop, you already know that spelling struggles don’t stay on the paper. They leak into the air of the house. They affect homework time, bedtime, mornings, and the way a child walks into school. They affect the way a parent speaks, even when they are trying to be patient. They affect siblings who learn, quietly, which child is “good at school” and which child is “the hard one.” They affect the child’s sense of who they are.

And because spelling is so often treated as a basic skill, the emotional toll has a particular sharpness to it.

When a child struggles in math, adults often say, “Math is hard.” When a child struggles in sports, adults say, “It takes practice.” But when a child struggles with spelling, the world tends to say, “Be more careful.” “Slow down.” “Try harder.” “You knew this.” It frames the struggle as a choice.

That framing is the seed of shame.

Shame is different from disappointment. Disappointment says, “That didn’t work.” Shame says, “I am the kind of person who can’t do this.” Shame is sticky. Shame spreads. Shame makes a child hide their work, avoid the task, rush to get it over with, or clamp down into perfectionism and panic. It also makes learning harder, because it pushes the nervous system into threat mode, the exact state where retrieval and orthographic mapping get worse.

By now, you can probably picture this cycle, because you’ve lived it.

The child misspells a word they “knew.” An adult reacts, sometimes with frustration, sometimes with a weary sigh, sometimes with a correction that is technically calm but emotionally hot. The child feels exposed. The

child’s body tightens. The brain that needs calm attention for mapping now has less of it. More errors happen. The adult tries to increase effort with more practice, more time, more repetition, maybe higher stakes. The child experiences that as proof that something is wrong with them. The temperature rises. And the very thing you want, spelling that sticks, moves farther away.

This is why lowering the temperature is not a soft, optional first step. It is a brain-based intervention.

A child cannot map words well while bracing for impact.

Think about what spelling demands: retrieve a precise sequence of letters, keep it in mind, hold it steady, and output it. If dyslexia is making the sound-to-letter bonds fragile, or dysgraphia is making the hand steal working memory, your child is already close to the edge of overload. Add the emotional load of being watched, corrected, compared, timed, or judged, and the system tips.

This is the part that is so unfair: many children who struggle with spelling are trying extremely hard to avoid being wrong.

They may ask for help constantly, not because they are helpless, but because they are trying to prevent the shame moment. They may refuse to start, because starting means risking the evidence on paper. They may rush and scribble and say, “I don’t care,” because caring has become expensive. They may melt down over a single word, because the word is not just a word anymore. It is a referendum on their intelligence, their effort, their goodness.

If you have ever heard your child say, “I’m just bad at spelling,” you have heard the emotional toll in one sentence. That sentence is not information about spelling. It is information about identity.

The tragedy is that the adults around them may agree without meaning to.

A child hears “You’re not careful” as “You are careless.”

A child hears “You’re rushing again” as “You are irresponsible.”

A child hears “We practiced this” as “You waste everyone’s time.”

A child hears “Other kids can do this” as “You are behind and everyone can see it.”

Even if you never say those exact meanings, children infer them. They are always reading the emotional weather.

And because spelling errors are visible, public, and permanent on the page, they carry a unique vulnerability. A child can answer a question out loud and move on. A misspelled word sits there in graphite and red ink. It can be circled, underlined, corrected, rewritten, graded, displayed. It can be laughed at by peers. It can be misunderstood by teachers. It can follow the child into the next grade as “poor spelling,” a trait rather than a skill.

For some kids, this becomes a form of anticipatory shame.

They begin to predict humiliation before it happens. They avoid writing. They use smaller words. They write less. They keep their sentences simple not because their thoughts are simple, but because simple words are safer. They choose “nice” instead of “beautiful.” “Big” instead of “enormous.” “Went” instead of “travelled.” Their vocabulary shrinks on paper. Their personality fades from their writing. Over time, they may start to believe that their thoughts are small too, because the page keeps telling that story.

This is also where the overlap between dyslexia and dysgraphia can multiply the emotional burden.

If your child has dyslexia, they may be working hard just to decide which letters to use. Each word is a gamble. They may already feel behind in a way they can’t name.

If your child has dysgraphia, they may be working hard just to make the pencil move correctly. They may already feel physically stressed by writing. If their hand hurts, or their letters won’t stay on the line, or their writing is criticized for neatness, the message becomes: even when you know it, you still fail.

When both are present, the child is often trapped between two impossible demands: “Spell it correctly” and “Write it neatly and quickly.” The adult may believe they are asking for something reasonable. The child experiences it as constant exposure.

It is worth saying directly: many of the tears are not about spelling. They are about what spelling has come to mean.

Spelling becomes the place where the child feels watched.

Spelling becomes the place where the child feels behind.

Spelling becomes the place where the child feels trapped in the same failure again and again.

Spelling becomes the place where the child feels like a disappointment.

And when a child is repeatedly asked to do a task that repeatedly ends in correction, the brain starts protecting itself. That protection can look like defiance, but underneath it is often grief. Grief is an appropriate response to trying hard and not getting the result you want, over and over, while people imply you are not trying.

Parents carry their own emotional toll, too, and it matters because children feel it.

You may feel dread when you open the folder. You may feel helpless because nothing seems to work. You may feel anger at the school system for using the same weekly test that keeps proving your child wrong. You may feel guilt that you don’t have the time, training, or energy to reinvent literacy at your kitchen table. You may feel embarrassed, especially if you recognize the struggle in yourself and you’ve spent a lifetime hiding your own spelling weaknesses.

And because parenting is intimate, your child may press exactly on that sore spot without intending to.

When a child melts down, a parent’s nervous system often mirrors it. You might find yourself saying something sharper than you meant. You might push more because you’re scared of them falling behind. You might get caught in the same trap the school is in: “If we can just get through this list, we’re okay.” The list becomes the emergency, even though the emergency is actually the child’s relationship with learning.

So lowering the temperature begins with a decision that feels small but is actually structural.

You decide that protecting your child’s sense of safety is part of the spelling plan, not a detour from it.

Safety does not mean you never correct mistakes. It does not mean you don’t practice. It does not mean you pretend the problem isn’t real. Safety means the child is not emotionally punished for having the wiring they have. Safety means mistakes are treated as information, not as evidence of a flawed self. Safety means your tone says, “We are on the same team.”

In practice, this can look like tiny shifts that change everything.

When your child misspells a word that “should be known,” you stop saying, “You know this.”

You say, “It didn’t stay mapped yet.”

You stop saying, “How many times do we have to go over this?”

You say, “Your brain needs a different kind of practice for this one.”

You stop saying, “Just look at it.”

You say, “Let’s connect the sounds to the letters again.”

You stop using the mistake as proof that practice failed.

You use the mistake as a clue about what part of the system needs support: sound clarity, grapheme choice, chunking, or output load.

This is not only kinder. It is more accurate. And accuracy is calming.

There is also a practical boundary that helps many families immediately: stop correcting everything.

If every piece of writing becomes a spelling lesson, writing becomes unsafe. A child cannot compose freely while simultaneously defending themselves from corrections. If dysgraphia is part of the picture, they cannot even form letters freely. The result is that the child stops writing, not because they have nothing to say, but because writing has become a place where they are constantly wrong.

So you choose a focus.

If the assignment is about ideas, you protect ideas.

If the assignment is about spelling practice, you keep it short, explicit, and winnable.

If the child is drafting, you let the draft be messy.

If the child is publishing, you offer help with editing, and you separate “let’s make this readable” from “let’s prove you can do it alone.”

Lowering the temperature also means changing what you celebrate.

A child who has been living in shame needs evidence of progress that is

not tied to perfection. If you only praise a perfect score, you teach the child that anything less is failure. But if you praise what actually builds mapping, you reinforce the real skill.

You can praise pausing and stretching sounds.

You can praise noticing a pattern.

You can praise using a tool without shame.

You can praise effort that is strategic rather than endless.

You can praise the child for returning to the task after a hard moment, because resilience is part of the long game.

And you can praise honesty. When a child says, “I don’t know,” instead of guessing wildly, that is not weakness. That is a child choosing accuracy over performance, which is exactly what mapping requires.

Perhaps the most important emotional shift is this: you stop treating spelling as the weekly measure of whether your child is okay.

Your child is not their spelling list.

Your parenting is not the Friday score.

The goal is not to win a test. The goal is to build a system.

And systems are built slowly, with repetition that is calm enough to store, explicit enough to map, and small enough not to overload working memory or the hand.

If this subchapter does only one thing, let it do this: let it give you permission to take the heat out of spelling before you take the errors out of spelling.

Because in the next sections of this chapter, we are going to talk about how to replace the Friday test mindset with small wins, and how to build a home environment where your child can practice without bracing for shame. But you can begin today, right now, by noticing the emotional toll for what it is: not drama, not defiance, not overreaction.

It is the predictable human response to repeated failure in a task that adults treat as basic.

When you see that clearly, compassion stops being a vague feeling and

becomes a practical strategy. You lower the temperature, and your child’s brain has a chance to do the work it was built to do: learn.

The Friday test is a strange little machine.

On the outside, it looks reasonable. A list comes home. You practice all week. A test happens. A score appears. Everyone knows where they stand. It feels orderly, measurable, familiar.

But if your child is dyslexic, dysgraphic, or simply overwhelmed by the load of spelling, the Friday test often becomes the exact opposite of what it claims to be. Instead of measuring learning, it measures short-term rehearsal. Instead of building confidence, it builds a weekly cycle of bracing and shame. Instead of strengthening the spelling system, it trains your child to treat words as temporary things you survive.

You can hear it in the way the week starts.

Monday: the list arrives. Your child’s shoulders go up before you’ve even said a word. Or they go flat, that quiet collapse that says, Here we go again.

Tuesday: you try to make it pleasant. You do the reasonable things. “Let’s sound them out.” “Let’s write them neatly.” “Let’s do five a day.” You are trying to help. Your child may even cooperate. Sometimes you have a good night and you think, Maybe this will be the week it clicks.

Wednesday: the fatigue hits. Your child begins to bargain. “Do I have to?” “Can we do it tomorrow?” You start feeling your own tightness because you know the clock is moving.

Thursday: panic practice. More repetition. More urgency. You might hear yourself say, “We have to get ready for the test,” as if Friday is a cliff.

Friday: the performance. In school, the child’s nervous system is already running hot from being watched, timed, compared. At home, you may be holding your breath for the score because the score is treated like proof that everything is fine.

And then Monday comes, and the word vanishes in real writing, and the child says, “But I knew it,” and you feel the whole week collapse behind you like a sandcastle.

If you want to lower the temperature, you do not start by trying to make the Friday test less stressful. You start by replacing what it represents.

Because the Friday test is not only an assessment. It is a mindset. It teaches the child that spelling is about being right on demand. It teaches the parent that progress is a number. It teaches everyone that forgetting is failure.

A struggling learner does not need more ways to fail publicly. They need more ways to win privately, steadily, in a form that actually builds the spelling system we talked about in Chapter 1: orthographic mapping.

So what does “replace the Friday test with small wins” mean in a real home?

It means you stop organizing your week around performance and start organizing it around skill-building.

A small win is not a gold star for effort. It is a winnable unit of learning that your child can complete accurately enough, calmly enough, and often enough that the brain can store it.

A small win is also specific. It does not mean, “Be a better speller.” It means, “Map this one sound.” Or, “Hold this one chunk.” Or, “Write this one word from memory without looking.” Or, “Use a tool to get your idea down without melting.”

Small wins are how you get out of the sticky-note memory trap.

Here is the first shift, and it matters more than it sounds: you stop treating a perfect score as the goal.

A perfect score can happen for the wrong reasons. It can happen because your child rehearsed the list in the car, because the words were familiar, because the test format was predictable, because the nervous system stayed calm for five minutes, because short-term working memory held just long enough.

None of that is bad. But it does not necessarily mean the words are mapped into long-term memory.

When you chase perfect scores, you quietly teach your child that being wrong is dangerous. You also teach them to rely on cramming, because cramming is the fastest route to a good Friday number. Then Monday happens, and the child learns the other lesson: it doesn’t matter how hard I work, it still disappears.

Small wins teach a different lesson: the kind of practice we do is what makes it stick.

So what might a small win look like this week?

It might look like choosing three words, not ten.

Or choosing one pattern, not a whole list.

Or practicing one irregular word like said by anchoring what is regular and flagging what is surprising, instead of trying to brute-force memorize it.

It might look like taking handwriting out of the equation for part of the practice, if dysgraphia is stealing your child’s attention. The small win could be spelling with letter tiles or typing the word correctly after mapping it orally. The goal is not to avoid handwriting forever. The goal is to stop requiring the hand to do two hard jobs at once: form letters and hold spelling in mind.

It might look like building a win around retrieval, not copying.

Copying feels safe because the word is right there. But retrieval is the doorway to mapping. So the small win could be: “Look at the word. Now cover it. Now write it from memory.” If that’s too hard, make it smaller: “Look at the word. Cover it. Now tell me the first sound and the first letter.” Or: “Cover it and write just the tricky part.” Every one of those is a legitimate win because it is strengthening the pathway from memory to output.

Notice what happens emotionally when you aim for that kind of win.

The child is not trapped in an hour-long battle to get all ten words perfect.

They are doing a short, clear task with a finish line they can see.

And when they make a mistake, the mistake is not proof that the whole week is ruined. It is one clue: this part is not mapped yet. We adjust and try again.

The second shift is that you move from weekly judgment to daily evidence.

The Friday test is a once-a-week snapshot taken under pressure. It is not a good measure for a child whose access fluctuates with stress, fatigue, and working memory load. Small wins are daily. They let you build a trail of proof that the system is changing.

You can keep it simple. You do not need charts and stickers unless your child loves them. Evidence can be as quiet as noticing, “Yesterday they needed the word model. Today they didn’t.” Or, “Last week they guessed the vowel every time. Today they paused and used the pattern.” Or, “Two weeks ago they could only write one sentence before melting down. Today they dictated three and typed one.”

These are not sentimental victories. They are signs that capacity is increasing.

If you want a concrete way to do this without creating another test, use what we might call the calm check.

At the end of a short practice session, you choose one word, just one, and you ask for it again later, in a different moment. Not with a stern tone. Not with an audience. Just casually, as if you’re checking whether the brain filed it.

“Hey, can you write ship for me on this sticky note?” Or, “How do you spell said again? I’m curious what your brain remembers.”

If they get it right, you say, “There it is. That one is starting to stick.” If they miss it, you say, “Okay, not mapped yet. That’s information.” Then you move on. No sigh. No lecture. No extra twenty minutes. The calm check teaches your child that spelling is something you build, not something you get judged for.

The third shift is that you make the win about the process, not the product.

For a child who has been living in shame, process praise is not a participation trophy. It is a way to direct attention to the skills that actually create mapping.

You might say, “I noticed you slowed down and tapped the sounds. That’s how your brain stores it.”

Or, “You fixed it right away when it was wrong. That’s what makes it stick.”

Or, “You used the tool without hiding. That’s smart.”

Or, “You took a breath and tried again. That’s the long game.”

This matters because many struggling learners have learned to protect themselves by rushing or avoiding. Process praise rewards the opposite:

accurate attention.

And the fourth shift is the one that often surprises parents: you begin to detach your home from the school’s weekly ritual.

Some families can opt out of spelling tests formally through accommodations. Some cannot. Many are in a gray zone where the teacher is kind, but the system is the system. Regardless, you can change what the list means in your house.

You can say, plainly, “At school, they do a Friday test. That’s their system. At home, we’re building your spelling system. We are not going to make you miserable all week for a number that disappears on Monday.”

This sentence does not undermine the teacher. It protects the child. It also tells your child that you understand the difference between performance and learning, which is one of the most regulating gifts you can give.

Then you choose what you will do instead.

You might still look at the list, but you treat it like a menu, not a mandate. You pick the words that match what your child is learning in sound- pattern instruction. You skip the ones that are far beyond their current map, because practicing too-hard words is not grit. It is overload. Or you turn the list into pattern practice: “These words all have the same vowel team. Let’s map that team.” That turns a pile of random memorization into a system the brain can store.

You might also decide that your child will practice spelling for fifteen minutes a day, as we’ll lay out later in the book, and that the practice will be based on the next right step, not on the school’s pacing. That is not a refusal. It is a correction. You are correcting the mismatch between how the brain learns and how the system tests.

If you feel guilty reading that, notice what the guilt is attached to. Often it is attached to the fear that if you don’t chase the weekly list, your child will fall behind.

But the truth is that many children are already falling behind while chasing the list, because the list is not building the foundation. It is training survival.

Small wins build foundation.

And the irony is that when the foundation strengthens, the Friday test often improves anyway, because the child has more mapped words to pull from and less panic around being wrong.

One last piece: small wins have to be small enough to be real.

A win that requires your child to white-knuckle through tears is not a win. A win that costs you an hour and a fight is not a win. A win that leaves your child feeling stupid is not a win, even if the words are correct.

A real win leaves the nervous system steady enough that learning can be stored.

That is why the finish line matters. End the practice while your child is still okay. Stop one minute earlier than you think you should. Let the brain leave the table with a sense of “I can do this,” not “I survived this.”

Because the goal of replacing the Friday test is not merely to change how you measure spelling. It is to change what spelling feels like in your child’s body.

Not a cliff every Friday.

Not a weekly humiliation.

Not a test of goodness.

A series of small, winnable steps that build a real spelling system, the kind that shows up on Monday without you having to hold your breath.

In the next section, we’re going to take this same idea and widen it into your whole home environment, because small wins don’t happen only in the practice minutes. They happen when the house becomes a place where mistakes are safe, tools are allowed, and your child stops living braced for the red pen.

A safe, supportive home learning environment is not a Pinterest corner with perfectly sharpened pencils. It is not a silent house, a parent who never gets impatient, or a child who always cooperates.

It is something much simpler and much more powerful: a place where your child’s nervous system does not have to brace for impact.

In the last section we talked about replacing the Friday test with small wins. That shift works best when the environment around the practice stops feeling like a courtroom. Your child does not learn spelling best

when they feel evaluated. They learn spelling best when they feel protected enough to take small risks, make a mistake, correct it, and try again without losing dignity.

And because you are doing this at home, you have an advantage the classroom often doesn’t: you can change the emotional weather quickly. You can take the heat out of the air even if the school system is still running on weekly lists and red pen logic.

Start with this: your home does not have to mimic school to support school.

Many parents try to help by recreating a classroom at the kitchen table. They sit the child upright. They say, “Let’s focus.” They keep the list in front of them. They correct every error. They push through tears because, “This has to get done.” It’s a reasonable instinct. But for a child living in the Friday-to-Monday loop, school-like conditions often trigger the very threat mode that blocks retrieval and orthographic mapping.

So the first building block of a supportive environment is a clear boundary between learning and judgment.

You can say it out loud, because children need language for what they are feeling. Try something like, “This is practice. Practice is where we make mistakes on purpose so the brain can learn. No one gets in trouble for mistakes in practice.” Or, if your child has been burned enough that they don’t believe you yet, be even more specific: “I’m not going to sigh. I’m not going to lecture. If it’s wrong, we just fix it and move on.”

Then you have to keep the promise. Not perfectly. Just steadily enough that your child starts to trust the pattern.

That leads to the next building block: predictable routines and predictable tone.

Many struggling learners do not melt down because of the spelling word itself. They melt down because they can’t predict how the next ten minutes will feel. Will it be quick? Will it turn into an hour? Will I be corrected for every letter? Will my parent get frustrated? Will I be told I knew this? Will my hand hurt? Will I end up ashamed?

Predictability calms the brain. Calm is fuel for mapping.

This is why the fifteen-minute routine later in the book matters so much, but you can begin right now with a smaller version: begin and end the same way.

Beginning: “We’re going to do a short practice. Then we’re done.”

End: “That’s it. We’re stopping while your brain is still okay.”

If your child asks, “How long will this take?” answer with a real time limit. Not “until you get it.” Not “until it’s perfect.” Perfection is a moving target, and your child knows it. A child who is afraid of an endless session cannot relax into learning.

You can even use a timer, not as a threat, but as a safety net. “When the timer beeps, we stop.” You are not trying to trap your child into productivity. You are trying to prove that practice has edges.

Now consider the physical environment, because it affects the nervous system more than we admit.

Some children do best at a table with feet supported, good lighting, and fewer visual distractions. Some children do best on the floor with a clipboard. Some do best standing at a counter. Many dysgraphic children do better when their body is stabilized: feet on the floor, chair at the right height, paper positioned so the wrist can move comfortably, not twisted into a cramped posture.

If your child tightens their grip and presses hard, they are already in a fight with the pencil. Reduce friction wherever you can. Try a softer pencil grip, a thicker pencil, or even a felt-tip pen if it makes writing smoother. Use lined paper that helps with letter height if that is a stress point. If handwriting is a major bottleneck, build in permission to switch channels: letter tiles, typing, or dictation for part of the practice. Remember what we said in Chapter 3: tools are ramps when they match the bottleneck.

Just as important as the furniture is the audience.

Many children can take risks with one safe adult and freeze when siblings are listening. If possible, protect practice from being a public performance. That might mean doing it while a younger sibling is occupied, or stepping into a quieter room, or simply saying to the household, “This is practice time. No comments.” Not because your child is fragile, but because the goal is to build a mapping system, not to test courage.

Now let’s talk about correction, because correction is where most home environments accidentally heat up.

In a supportive environment, mistakes are handled as information. That

does not mean you ignore them. It means you correct them in a way that the brain can store, without adding shame.

Here is one helpful rule: correct immediately, briefly, and calmly.

Immediate, because the brain practices what it just did.

Brief, because long explanations can feel like scolding and can overload working memory.

Calm, because threat mode blocks retrieval.

So it might sound like this:

Child writes sed for said.

You say, “You mapped the s and the d. Good. This middle is the surprising part. It’s ai. Let’s write said together once. Now cover and you try.”

That’s it. No “We did this yesterday.” No “How many times.” No extra heat.

If your child makes the same mistake again, treat it as a sign that the word is still not mapped, not as proof that your child is not listening. Repeat the same correction pattern with the same calm. Your consistency is what makes the environment safe.

Another rule: separate correction from composition.

When your child is writing something meaningful, a story, a science sentence, a journal entry, the goal is ideas. If you correct every spelling error in that moment, you teach the child that writing equals being wrong. Many struggling writers respond by writing less, using smaller words, and keeping their thoughts trapped in their head where they can’t be judged.

So decide, ahead of time, what kind of writing this is.

If it’s drafting, let it be messy. You can say, “Just get it down. Spelling can be wrong in drafts.”

If it’s practice, keep it narrow: “Right now we’re mapping these three words.”

If it’s a final copy, you can help them edit without making it a character moment. “Let’s use spell-check. Let’s look up this word. Let’s fix it

together so your work says what you mean.”

This separation is part of building safety. It tells your child, “There are times when we focus on spelling, and times when we protect your voice.”

Now we need to talk about the parent’s nervous system, because it sets the temperature more than any strategy.

You have your own Friday-to-Monday story. You have your own fatigue. You may have your own old shame if spelling was hard for you too. You may be carrying fear: What if they fall behind? What if the teacher thinks I’m not doing enough? What if this is forever?

Your child can feel that fear, even if you never say it.

One of the kindest things you can do is to build a small pause for yourself before practice begins. It can be as simple as one breath and one sentence in your head: This is wiring. This is skill-building. This is not a moral emergency.

Then make your tone match that truth.

If you can’t, if you’re too depleted that day, it is better to do less than to do more with heat. Remember the definition of a real win from the last section: a win leaves the nervous system steady enough to store learning. A tense, extended session can undo the very thing you’re trying to build.

In a supportive environment, it is allowed to stop.

You can say, “We’re out of capacity. We’ll try again tomorrow.” This teaches a child an essential life skill: noticing overload and responding with a plan, not with shame. It also protects the relationship, which matters because you are not only teaching spelling. You are teaching what learning feels like.

Another building block is choice, but not the kind that turns into negotiation.

Choice lowers threat. It gives the child a sense of control, which is often what they lost in school experiences that felt like repeated exposure.

Offer two acceptable options: “Do you want to spell with tiles or with the pencil first?” “Do you want to sit at the table or on the floor?” “Do you want to do practice before snack or after snack?” These are small choices, but they send a big message: you have agency here.

Agency is calming.

Now let’s bring back a concept from earlier chapters: recognition versus retrieval.

A supportive environment is designed for retrieval without humiliation.

That means you build in covering the word, trying it from memory, and treating the attempt as data. It also means you normalize blanks. Some children freeze because they believe “I don’t know” is not allowed. Make it allowed.

You can say, “It’s okay to say ‘I don’t know.’ That’s how we find the next step.” Then you supply the next step: “Let’s tap the sounds.” Or, “Let’s write the first sound.” Or, “Let’s look, cover, and try again.”

A child who can safely admit uncertainty is a child whose brain is available to learn. A child who has to perform certainty is a child who will guess, hide, or melt down.

Finally, build a culture of tools without guilt.

If your child has dysgraphia, typing or dictation may be the difference between showing knowledge and being trapped by the hand. If your child has dyslexia, spell-check and text-to-speech can reduce daily humiliation while the mapping system is built. Using a tool is not cheating. It is choosing the right channel for the job.

You can say, plainly, “We use tools so your brain can focus on ideas. Handwriting and spelling practice are separate times. Tools are allowed.”

This doesn’t mean you never practice handwriting or spelling. It means you stop making every piece of schoolwork carry the full weight of remediation.

A safe home learning environment is not a place where everything is easy. It is a place where hard things are made doable.

It is where you replace “You knew this” with “Not mapped yet.”

It is where you replace “Fix it” with “Let’s try it again, smaller.”

It is where you replace endless practice with short, winnable practice.

And over time, something shifts. Not overnight, not like a movie montage,

but quietly.

Your child walks to the table with less dread.

They take more risks.

They recover faster from mistakes.

They start to believe a new story: “This is hard for my brain, but there’s a way through.”

That belief is not sentimental. It is the foundation of everything that comes next in this book. Because once the temperature drops, your child’s brain can finally do the work that spelling has been demanding all along: focused attention, clear sound-to-letter connections, and steady retrieval practice that builds real, durable maps.

Chapter 5·The Multisensory Method — Orton Gillingham, Made Free

When people hear “multisensory,” they often picture glitter, shaving cream, and a child happily tracing letters in a tray of sand.

Sometimes that is part of it. But if that is all you take from the word, you will miss the real reason multisensory structured literacy helps spelling stick for the exact child who keeps losing words between Friday and Monday.

Multisensory is not a craft category. It is not “make it fun.” It is not “use your hands because kids like hands-on activities.” And it is definitely not “add movement so they burn energy.”

Multisensory learning, in the Orton-Gillingham sense, means something very specific: you teach the brain to bind the parts of written language together by engaging more than one pathway at the same time, on purpose, in a tightly coordinated way.

Remember the relay race model from Chapter 2. Reading and spelling require a handoff between speech sounds, letter patterns, meaning, and output. For many learners, that baton handoff becomes efficient with typical instruction. For dyslexic learners, the sound-to-print handoff is often the shaky point. For dysgraphic learners, the handoff to the hand can be the shaky point. Multisensory instruction is how we strengthen the handoffs so the system stops dropping the baton under load.

Here is the simplest definition you can keep in your head:

Multisensory means the child sees it, says it, hears it, and does it, all in one small moment, with attention.

Not “sees it” on Monday and “writes it” on Tuesday and “says it” on Friday. Not “copies it five times while thinking about something else.” We are talking about a single, connected event: the sound, the symbol, and the movement linked together so the brain can form a durable map.

That is why multisensory helps orthographic mapping.

Orthographic mapping, as we described earlier, is not visual memorization. It is the brain bonding phonemes to graphemes and tying that bond to meaning, so the word becomes retrievable later without a sticky-note rehearsal. When a child is struggling, the problem is often that the bond is weak, inconsistent, or overloaded by too many demands at

once.

Multisensory instruction does two things at the same time:

It strengthens the bond by giving the brain a clearer, louder signal.

It reduces the chance of losing the signal by keeping attention anchored in the task.

That second part matters more than most people realize. Many struggling learners can look directly at a word and still not store it, because “looking” is not the same as mapping. Looking can be passive. Mapping is active. Mapping requires the child to notice what sound is in the word, what letters represent it, and what their mouth and hand are doing as they produce it.

So what does this look like in practice?

Imagine you are teaching the sound /m/ and the letter m. A multisensory moment might look like this:

The child says, “/m/,” feeling their lips close.

They look at m.

They write m while saying “/m/” again.

They might trace it, sky-write it, or tap it with a finger, but the point is not the air-writing itself. The point is that the sound is being paired with the symbol while the body is doing a consistent movement.

The body becomes part of the memory hook.

This is especially powerful for kids whose working memory overloads easily. When the body is engaged in a purposeful way, it can act like a stabilizer. It gives the brain another anchor, another way to hold the information steady long enough to store it.

Now zoom out to spelling a word, because this is where parents often feel the most desperate.

Let’s take a simple word like ship. A multisensory spelling routine might be:

Say the word: “ship.”

Tap the sounds: /sh/ /i/ /p/ (tapping fingers, tapping the table, or tapping arm segments, whatever is comfortable and consistent).

Say each sound again while writing the matching grapheme: sh, i, p.

Then read what you wrote: “ship.”

Notice what is happening here. The child is not copying a visual shape. They are building the word from sound to print, and then confirming it back from print to sound. That is the mapping loop.

This is also where multisensory learning is different from simply “making spelling tactile.”

If a child writes ship in shaving cream but they are not connecting the sounds to the letters as they do it, you have a sensory experience but not necessarily a mapping experience. It might be fun, and fun is not bad, especially if you are lowering the temperature from Chapter 4. But the goal is not novelty. The goal is an accurate, repeated connection.

Multisensory is also not automatically helpful if it adds too much stimulation.

Some children love sensory materials. Some children get dysregulated by them. If your child becomes silly, distracted, or overwhelmed by the sensory part, the brain is no longer attending to the sound-symbol bond. Remember: mapping requires attention. If the sensory element steals attention, it defeats the purpose.

So if you have tried the sand tray and it turned into chaos, that is not a failure. It is data. Use a quieter sensory channel. A finger on the table. A marker on a whiteboard. Letter tiles. A simple tap-and-write routine. Multisensory does not have to mean messy.

In fact, for many families, the most effective multisensory work looks almost boring from the outside. It is calm. It is repetitive. It is small.

That is a feature, not a flaw.

Now let’s address the question many parents silently ask: “Why can’t my child just learn the normal way?”

Because “the normal way,” for spelling, often relies on two assumptions that do not hold for dyslexic and dysgraphic learners.

Assumption one: if you see a word enough times, you will remember it.

We already dismantled the memorization myth in Chapter 1. Many struggling spellers do see the words. They can even recognize them. Recognition is not retrieval. If the child cannot reliably retrieve the spelling from memory while generating writing, it isn’t mapped yet, no matter how many times they copied it.

Assumption two: the child has enough working memory to hold the sounds, choose letters, keep them in order, and also form the letters neatly and quickly.

This is where dyslexia and dysgraphia collide. If the sound side is shaky, the child struggles to choose the right grapheme. If the motor side is demanding, the child loses the sequence mid-word. Multisensory instruction helps because it slows the process down and makes the invisible steps visible, with supports in place.

It is not babying. It is making the brain’s job possible.

If you have a child who says, “I know it, I just can’t write it,” multisensory learning is also one way to separate the jobs, which we promised in Chapter 3.

You can build spelling with letter tiles or with finger tracing before you require handwriting. That lets the child practice the mapping without the output bottleneck swallowing all their attention. Then, later, you can add handwriting as a separate skill, not as the gatekeeper to spelling practice.

And if your child has dyslexia, the multisensory emphasis on sound is not optional. It is the point. It trains the sound system to become clearer and more stable, so that the mapping bonds can form.

Here is another way to think about it: multisensory instruction gives the brain more than one path back to the answer.

When a word is truly mapped, it is stored in a network, not in a single fragile thread. The child can access it through sound, through pattern, through meaning, and through the feel of writing it. If one route is weak on a given day, another route can help.

That matters because your child is not the same child every day.

Some days they slept well. Some days they didn’t. Some days the nervous system is calm. Some days it is still braced from school. Some days the hand is tired. Some days it isn’t.

Multisensory learning builds resilience into the system.

Now, one more clarification, because “multisensory” has been diluted by marketing.

People sometimes use the term to describe anything that involves movement. “Jump on the letters!” “Throw a beanbag at the word!” Again, that can be playful, and play can lower the temperature. But Orton- Gillingham multisensory instruction is not random movement layered on top of weak instruction. It is structured movement tied directly to the language.

The movement has to mean something.

Tapping has a purpose: it marks phonemes.

Tiles have a purpose: they externalize graphemes so working memory has less to hold.

Tracing has a purpose: it stabilizes letter formation while the sound is spoken, building the sound-symbol link.

Even “sky-writing” has a purpose when it is done slowly, with the child naming the strokes and saying the sound, because it strengthens the motor plan without the pressure of tiny pencil control.

If the activity does not strengthen a sound-symbol connection, it may still be fine, but it is not the core of the method.

And this is where the “made free” promise starts to show itself.

You do not need a special kit. You do not need a specialized classroom. You do not need to buy sensory bins or fancy programs. You need a few simple tools and a clear routine:

Your voice.

Your child’s voice.

A way to write (paper, whiteboard, chalk, even a finger on the table).

A way to build words if handwriting is a bottleneck (tiles made from paper squares work; so do Scrabble tiles; so do magnetic letters if you already have them).

And most importantly, a shared understanding that the goal is mapping,

not performing.

If you keep the temperature low from Chapter 4, multisensory practice becomes something your child can approach without bracing. It becomes, “We are going to build the word together,” not “Prove you can do it.”

In the next part of this chapter, we’re going to talk about Orton- Gillingham principles for families, because multisensory is only one piece. The method works because it is multisensory and structured: explicit, sequential, cumulative, and designed for the exact learner who needs more repetition without more shame.

But for now, hold on to this:

Multisensory learning is not a gimmick. It is how you help a struggling brain bind sound, symbol, and movement into one stable memory. It is how you turn spelling practice from copying into mapping. And for the child whose spelling won’t stick, it is often the first time spelling begins to feel less like a weekly judgment and more like a skill their brain can actually build.

Orton-Gillingham can sound like a private-school password. Families hear it and picture expensive tutoring, specialized schools, and a kind of instruction that must require a certificate just to pronounce.

What matters for you, at your kitchen table, is not the brand name. It is the set of principles underneath it. Orton-Gillingham is not a single program. It is a way of teaching reading and spelling that was built for the exact learner we’ve been talking about: the child for whom spelling won’t stick through normal memorization routines, the child whose working memory overloads, the child whose brain needs clearer handoffs in the relay race between sound, print, meaning, and output.

Those principles are what you can borrow. You do not need to reproduce a full clinic. You need a handful of guardrails that keep practice effective and keep the temperature low.

Here are the principles, translated into family language.

First: Be explicit. Say the quiet parts out loud.

Most classroom spelling assumes that children will infer the system. They’ll “pick up” that sh says /sh/. They’ll notice that silent e changes the vowel. They’ll absorb that certain patterns show up again and again.

Many children do.

Your child may not. Not because they aren’t smart, but because their brain is not automatically extracting the code from exposure. In Chapter 2 we called this wiring, not willpower. In Chapter 1 we called out the memorization myth. Orton-Gillingham begins by removing the guessing game.

Explicit means you do not hint and hope. You tell.

“This sound is /sh/. These two letters, s and h, work together as a team to say /sh/.”

“This word is said. It looks like it should sound like ‘sayed,’ but in English we say ‘sed.’ The middle is the surprising part, and we remember it.”

When you say the quiet parts out loud, your child stops feeling like spelling is a trick. They start to feel like it is a system with names and reasons. That reduces shame immediately, because shame thrives in mystery.

Second: Be systematic and sequential. Teach one new thing at a time, on purpose.

Many spelling lists are organized by curriculum pacing, not by a child’s readiness. You can get a list with “light, night, right, eight, weight, freight” and it’s supposed to be one tidy lesson on a sound. But that list actually contains multiple patterns and multiple memory demands. A struggling learner can’t easily sort that out. The brain hears, “Here are ten traps. Good luck.”

Orton-Gillingham says: we choose the next small step, and we teach it in an order that builds.

That usually means you start with sound-to-letter connections that are consistent and high-use, then you add complexity. You do not jump to vowel teams if short vowels are not stable. You do not stack three new ideas in the same week and then blame the child for forgetting.

Sequential also means you respect the size of working memory. If Chapter 1 gave you the image of sticky-note learning, this is the way out of it. The brain cannot store what it cannot hold steady. So you make the learning small enough to hold.

For a family, this might sound like, “This week we’re working on the /sh/ team and a few words that use it.” Not “Here’s the whole list, plus the bonus words, plus a paragraph to copy.”

Third: Be cumulative. Review is not a detour. Review is the method.

Struggling spellers often need two kinds of repetition.

They need repetition across days so the mapping has time to consolidate.

They need repetition across contexts so retrieval becomes flexible, not tied to one practice page.

Orton-Gillingham builds review into every lesson. Not as a punishment, not as “we’re still doing this because you forgot,” but as a normal part of learning.

At home, cumulative can be as simple as beginning practice with two or three quick review items before you add anything new.

“Before we do today’s new word, let’s write ship and shop from memory.”

Or, if handwriting is heavy for your child, “Before we do today’s new word, build ship with tiles and read it.”

This review does two things. It strengthens old maps, and it keeps your child from feeling like everything disappears. A child living in the Friday- to-Monday loop needs proof that something is staying. Cumulative review creates that proof.

Fourth: Be diagnostic and responsive. Let the child’s errors tell you what to do next.

In many traditional routines, an error means, “Do it again.” Or worse, “You didn’t try.”

Orton-Gillingham treats an error as information. It asks, “What kind of error is this?”

Did your child write sed for said? That’s not random. It tells you the child is relying on sound, which is a reasonable strategy in a sound-based spelling system. The fix is not “be careful.” The fix is to teach that said is an irregular high-frequency word with a surprising middle, and to map it with attention.

Did your child write shp for ship? That suggests they may be dropping the vowel under working memory load, or rushing past the middle sound. The fix might be to tap the sounds slowly and make the vowel visible and unavoidable.

Did your child spell it correctly out loud but not on paper? That points you back to Chapter 3. The motor load may be disrupting the sequence. The fix might be to practice spelling with tiles first, then write only the word once, or to let the child type during the mapping practice and save handwriting for a separate, shorter lane.

Diagnostic teaching is what keeps practice from turning into endless repetition. You’re not doing “more.” You’re doing “more precise.”

Fifth: Be multisensory, but also be coordinated.

You just learned in the last section that multisensory is not glitter. It is binding sound, symbol, and movement in one moment with attention.

Orton-Gillingham uses multisensory work as the delivery system for explicit, sequential instruction. This is important: multisensory is not the principle by itself. It is the way the principle lands in the brain.

For families, coordinated multisensory often looks like a small, consistent routine:

Say the word.

Tap the sounds.

Write the letters while saying the sounds.

Read the word back.

If your child is young, you might add a quick meaning hook: “Ship is a boat. Picture it.” If your child is older, you might add a morphology hook later, as we’ll explore in Chapter 8, but you can begin with meaning even now: mapped words stick better when they are tied to something real.

The coordination matters because it prevents the common home-practice problem: doing five different activities that never quite connect. One day you trace, one day you copy, one day you play a game, one day you write on the whiteboard, but the child is never required to bind sound to symbol to meaning in a tight loop.

Keep the loop. Change the surface if you need variety, but keep the loop.

Sixth: Aim for mastery, not coverage.

This one is the hardest for parents, because it rubs against school pacing

and parental fear.

Coverage says, “We did the whole list. We’re done.”

Mastery says, “We learned a smaller amount, but it actually stuck.”

For a child whose spelling won’t stick, mastery is the only path that doesn’t create more shame. If you cover ten words quickly and your child forgets nine, you didn’t move forward. You added heat.

Orton-Gillingham moves forward when the child is ready, which often means fewer words, more repetition, and more success. The irony is that this usually speeds up progress over time, because the foundation stops crumbling under the next layer.

You can tell you’re aiming for mastery when your child can retrieve the spelling after a pause, in a calm check like we described in Chapter 4, without staring at the model.

Mastery is not perfection under pressure. It is stability under normal conditions.

Seventh: Control the difficulty. Reduce load so the brain can learn.

This is where Orton-Gillingham quietly protects working memory.

You control difficulty by limiting the number of new items, by choosing words that match the pattern you’re teaching, and by separating tasks that overload your child when combined.

If dysgraphia is present, you do not require long handwritten lists as the main way to practice spelling. That is asking the hand to dominate the session. If dyslexia is present, you do not skip sound work and hope the child will memorize visually. That is asking the weakest link to carry the load.

Control difficulty also means you slow down on purpose. Not with a scolding tone, not “Slow down!” as a criticism, but slow down as a strategy.

“Let’s do this one like a scientist. We’re going to look at what sounds are in it.”

Your child may have spent years hearing “slow down” as a complaint. You’re going to turn it into support.

Eighth: Build automaticity through retrieval, not through copying.

This one connects directly to Chapter 1.

Copying can be calming, and it can be useful for handwriting practice, but it is a weak tool for spelling memory because it leans on recognition. Orton-Gillingham builds memory by making the child retrieve.

Look, cover, write, check.

Say it, tap it, write it from memory.

Build it with tiles without the model.

Then check and correct immediately.

This is also where you protect your child from the humiliation loop. Retrieval practice is supposed to include errors. Errors are not proof of failure. They are part of strengthening the map. The only rule is that the error gets corrected accurately and calmly, right away, so the brain practices the right form.

Ninth: Keep the lesson calm and short. The nervous system is part of instruction.

Orton-Gillingham in a clinic can be longer, but at home, your superpower is consistency. You are not trying to win an hour of compliance. You are trying to build a daily habit that does not trigger threat mode.

If Chapter 4 taught us anything, it’s that threat mode steals the attention mapping requires. So one of the most faithful ways to apply Orton- Gillingham principles at home is simply to end early.

Stop while the child is still okay.

Stop while you are still kind.

Stop with a win still warm in the child’s body.

That is not indulgence. That is brain science.

If you put these principles together, you can feel how different the experience becomes.

Instead of “Here’s your list, don’t mess up,” the message is, “We’re going to build your spelling system, one small brick at a time.”

Instead of “You knew this,” it becomes, “Not mapped yet. Let’s map it.”

Instead of “Try harder,” it becomes, “Let’s make the task smaller so your brain can store it.”

This is what Orton-Gillingham really offers families: a way out of randomness. A way out of weekly humiliation. A way out of treating spelling like a character trait.

In the next section, we’re going to get even more practical about making multisensory work at home without cost, because you don’t need special materials to follow these principles. You need a simple set of tools and a routine that stays the same even on hard days. That’s where the “made free” promise becomes real: not a watered-down version of good instruction, but the parts that matter most, rebuilt for fifteen unhurried minutes at your kitchen table.

When families hear “Orton-Gillingham,” they often assume there must be a kit.

A box of tiles with the right fonts. A set of sand trays. Special cards. A paid app. A trained tutor. A program that costs enough to make you feel like you’re choosing between your child’s future and your grocery budget.

But the reason Orton-Gillingham works is not because of branded materials. It works because of the principles you just learned: explicit, sequential, cumulative, diagnostic, multisensory, mastery over coverage, and calm retrieval practice. Those principles can live in very ordinary objects.

In fact, “made free” is not a cute promise in this book. It is a practical design requirement, because a method you cannot sustain is not a method. It becomes one more thing you tried that didn’t work, one more quiet failure you carry, one more reason your child thinks, “Even my parent couldn’t fix me.”

So let’s build a home version that keeps the real engine and skips the expensive packaging.

Start with the smallest possible toolkit. You need three categories of tools, and you can make all of them from what most homes already have.

One: something to write on and something to write with.

Paper and pencil work. A whiteboard works. A cheap notebook works. A

stack of scrap paper works. For some children, especially dysgraphic kids whose grip is tight and whose hand hurts, a smooth pen or marker on a whiteboard can lower the motor load immediately. If writing by hand is a hotspot, remember Chapter 3: we are not proving handwriting endurance. We are building a spelling system. Choose the writing surface that helps your child stay calm and accurate.

Two: something to cover the word.

This sounds silly until you remember the recognition versus retrieval problem from Chapter 1 and Chapter 4. A sticky note, an index card, a folded piece of paper, your hand. Anything that lets you do “look, cover, write, check” without turning it into a production.

Three: something to build words without handwriting, when needed.

You can use Scrabble tiles, magnetic letters, or letter tiles you cut from paper. If you want to make paper tiles, do not worry about making them pretty. A child does not need the right font. They need the right process.

Here is the simplest paper-tile setup: cut small squares from cardstock or even cereal boxes. Write one letter per square. Make multiple copies of common letters. If you don’t want to think about quantities, start with two of each consonant, four vowels, and a few common teams like sh, ch, th, ck. You can add as you go, because you are teaching sequentially. You do not need the entire English code in a jar on day one.

Those are the physical tools. Now we build the method.

The home version of multisensory structured literacy is not “lots of activities.” It is one small loop, repeated calmly, with tiny variations depending on your child’s needs that day.

The loop looks like this:

Say it.

Tap it.

Build it or write it while saying the sounds.

Read it.

Cover it.

Retrieve it.

Check it and correct it.

That is the engine. Everything else is optional.

Let’s make it concrete. Suppose you are working on the /sh/ team, like we discussed in 5.1. You choose three decodable words: ship, shop, fish. Not ten. Not a whole list that mixes patterns. Three is enough to build mastery without overload.

You begin with a review, because cumulative review is the method, not a detour.

“Before we do a new word, let’s do ship.”

You say, “ship.”

Your child repeats, “ship.”

You tap the sounds together: /sh/ /i/ /p/. This can be finger tapping, tapping on the table, or tapping along the arm. Use what is comfortable and consistent. You are not trying to entertain the body. You are using the body to mark sound units.

Then your child builds it with tiles or writes it, saying each sound as they place or write the letters.

“/sh/” place sh. “/i/” place i. “/p/” place p.

Then they slide a finger under it and read, “ship.”

Now cover it.

“Okay, now your brain retrieves it. Build it again without looking.”

If they make an error, you do not sigh. You do not say, “We just did that.” You do what we promised in Chapter 4: mistakes are information, not identity.

You say, “Good try. Let’s tap again: /sh/ /i/ /p/. What do we use for /sh/? Right, sh is a team.” Then you try again.

This is multisensory without cost. It is sound, symbol, and movement bound together, repeated with attention, short enough that the nervous system stays steady.

Now, parents often ask, “Do I have to do the tapping and the saying every time? Won’t it get annoying?”

Yes, it can feel repetitive. That’s the point. Repetition is how the brain builds the shortcut. The child who needs Orton-Gillingham principles is the child for whom the shortcut does not form automatically through casual exposure.

Also, repetition does not have to be loud or theatrical. Calm, matter-of- fact repetition is often more regulating than enthusiastic repetition. Think “scientist in a lab,” not “game show host.”

If your child has dysgraphia, you can keep the whole spelling lesson in tiles for a while, then add one single handwriting attempt at the end. That is not avoiding handwriting. That is controlling difficulty.

For example: build ship twice with tiles. Read it. Then say, “Now we write it one time.” One time. Not five. Not a whole line. You are adding handwriting as a final step, not using it as the main arena.

And if your child can spell it orally but not on paper, remember the clue from Chapter 3: the motor channel may be dropping the baton. Tiles let you strengthen the mapping without the hand stealing the working memory.
