If your child has dyslexia and the sound side is the bottleneck, you slow down even more on the sound work. You might spend an entire session on hearing and marking the sounds in words, even before you write.

Some children need to feel the sounds in their mouth. You can say, “Watch my mouth for /sh/. Feel your mouth. /sh/ is quiet air. Now /p/ is a pop.” This is still multisensory: the mouth is part of the sensory system, and it is free.

You can also use a mirror, which is another free tool if you have one. “Do you see how your lips close on /m/? That’s /m/.” This is not a gimmick. It is making an invisible sound visible, which helps a brain that struggles to segment phonemes.

Now let’s address the question that tends to trip families: “What do I teach? I’m not trained. I don’t know the order.”

You do not need to design a full scope and sequence to start helping your child. You need to follow two rules:

Teach what is decodable and consistent before what is complex and

exception-heavy.

Teach one new thing at a time, and choose words that mostly obey that one thing.

If you’re not sure whether a word is a good choice, use a simple filter: can my child reasonably sound this out with what they already know? If the answer is no, that word may belong later, or it may belong in the “irregular but high-frequency” category where you teach it explicitly as a special case, not as part of a pattern lesson.

This book will guide you into that progression in later chapters, especially Chapter 6, “One Sound at a Time,” and Chapter 7, “Chunk It.” For now, your job is not to cover everything. Your job is to build something real.

That brings us to the no-cost version of “word cards,” because many families assume they need printed materials.

You can make your own in thirty seconds. Take scrap paper. Write one word in large, clear letters. That’s it. On the back, you can write the sound taps with dots or slashes if it helps you remember how to guide the tapping.

But also: you don’t have to make cards at all. You can write the word once at the top of a page, use it as the model for “look,” then cover it and retrieve underneath.

The crucial part is not the card. It’s the cover.

Now let’s talk about keeping it calm at home, because cost isn’t only money. Cost is emotional energy.

The most expensive spelling plan is the one that requires you to fight your child every day.

So we design the practice to survive real life.

Make it short. We said earlier that at home your superpower is consistency, not duration. If you can do eight calm minutes, that can beat thirty heated minutes every time. “Stop while your brain is still okay” is not just a nice line. It’s a strategy that protects learning.

Make the finish line predictable. “We’re doing three words, two tries each, then we’re done.” When your child asks, “How long?” you can answer in a way that builds trust.

Keep the correction pattern the same. The same calm steps, every time: “Tap it. Say it. Fix it. Try again.” Predictability is regulating.

And choose a time when the household temperature is naturally lower, if you can. Many children cannot do sound work after a full day of school plus homework plus hunger. If your child is empty at 6 p.m., you are not failing. You are noticing capacity. A morning practice, a car practice using oral tapping, or a right-after-snack practice may work better.

If you want to add a “game” element without buying anything, keep it aligned with retrieval.

You can do “teacher and student” where the child teaches you the sound team you’re learning.

You can do “beat your yesterday” where the goal is not speed, but accuracy with fewer prompts.

You can do “mystery word” where you tap the sounds and the child builds the word with tiles, then reads it back.

None of those require purchases. The game is the brain work, wearing a friendlier outfit.

One more no-cost tool matters deeply for struggling learners: permission.

Permission to use the right channel.

If dysgraphia is active, let some spelling practice happen without handwriting so the child can actually attend to mapping.

If your child is old enough to type, a shared document on a device you already own can become a spelling notebook. You say the word, the child types it while saying the sounds, then reads it back. Typing is not a moral issue. It is an output channel. Chapter 9 will give you a fuller framework for “tools, not crutches,” but you can begin the guilt-free mindset now: using a different channel to reduce load is part of controlling difficulty, and controlling difficulty is part of good instruction.

The overall message to your child stays the same, day after day: “We’re building your spelling system.”

Not performing.

Not cramming.

Not surviving the Friday list.

Building.

And you can do that with a pencil, scrap paper, a few homemade tiles, and a calm, consistent loop.

The expensive part of spelling support has never been the materials. The expensive part is the expertise that tells you what to do and the gentleness that keeps the nervous system open enough to learn. You are building both, one small session at a time.

In the next chapter, we’re going to make the “what to teach next” piece much clearer by committing to one sound at a time, in a decodable, sequential progression that avoids overload on purpose. But you don’t have to wait for that chapter to start. You can start today with the free version: a small set of decodable words, a cover card, a tapping routine, and the calm correction that turns errors into information instead of shame. That is multisensory made real, and made sustainable, in an ordinary home.

Chapter 6·One Sound at a Time — Decodable,

Sequential, and Never Overwhelming

Decodable words are the place where struggling spellers finally get to feel what “fair” feels like.

Up to now, much of what your child has been asked to do has been a blend of three different jobs: hear the sounds in the word, choose the right letters for those sounds, and then hold the whole thing steady long enough to get it onto paper. When the list is full of words that don’t match what your child knows, the task becomes a guessing contest. When the list is full of words that require patterns your child hasn’t been taught, the task becomes a memorization contest. And when dysgraphia is riding along, it can become a handwriting endurance contest on top of everything else.

Decodable words remove the rigged parts of the game.

A decodable word is a word your child can spell (and usually read) using the sound-to-letter knowledge they have been explicitly taught so far. Not all-at-once knowledge. Not “what they should know by this grade.” Not “what they’ve seen a hundred times.” The knowledge they can actually access and use.

That last phrase matters, because access is the whole story of this book. Many children have been exposed to the code, but exposure is not mastery. A word is only truly decodable for your child if it can be built with the tools currently in their hand: the sounds they can hear, the graphemes they can retrieve, and a working memory load that does not tip them into panic.

You can feel the difference immediately at the table.

With a random spelling list, you might say, “Spell night,” and your child’s eyes do that quick scan for danger. Is this one of the words where the vowel does something weird? Is there a silent letter? Is there a trick? They may guess, freeze, or bargain. Even if they have seen the word many times, their brain has learned that spelling is full of traps.

With decodable words, the message shifts to, “You can do this with what you already know.”

That message lowers the temperature before you even start.

Decodable does not mean “easy.” It means “possible.” It means the

practice is set up so that the brain can focus on mapping instead of surviving.

Remember the engine we built in Chapter 5: say it, tap it, build or write it, read it, cover it, retrieve it, check and correct. That loop only works as intended if the word is within reach. If the word is too complex, your child can still participate in the loop, but the loop becomes a rehearsal of confusion. The brain practices uncertainty. It practices guessing. It practices failure. And that is the opposite of what we want.

Decodable words are the building blocks because they let you construct a real spelling system in the right order: one sound at a time, one pattern at a time, with enough success that the brain stays open for storage.

Here is what decodable looks like in real life.

If your child has been taught short a, short i, and the consonants m, s, t, p, then words like sat, sip, map, and tip are decodable. Your child can tap the sounds and choose letters that match. The words don’t require a silent e or a vowel team or an unusual spelling choice. They are clean practice for the mapping bonds you are trying to build.

If your child has been taught that sh is a team for /sh/ (like we used in Chapter 5), then ship, shop, and fish become decodable. Not because they are baby words, but because they are honest words. They behave the way the code says they should behave at this stage.

And honesty is a gift to a struggling learner.

This is where many parents worry: “But doesn’t my child need to practice real words? Doesn’t English have exceptions? What about the words they actually use at school?”

Yes. And we will handle those, especially the high-frequency irregular words that show up everywhere. But you do not build a house by starting with the roof. You build it by laying a foundation that can hold weight.

Decodable words are foundation work.

They give your child repetition on the most essential skill: hearing the sounds in a word and attaching the correct letters to those sounds, in order, without overload. That is the heart of orthographic mapping. It is the doorway out of sticky-note learning. And it is exactly what many struggling learners have not had enough of, because typical spelling lists are organized around weekly themes rather than around a carefully built sequence.

There is another reason decodable words matter, and it’s easy to miss: decodable words let you be truly diagnostic without getting lost.

In Chapter 5 we talked about Orton-Gillingham being diagnostic and responsive: errors tell you what to do next. That only works if the word itself is not the problem.

If you ask a child to spell laugh and they write laf, what did you learn? You learned that English is complicated and that your child is relying on sound. That’s not a useful diagnosis at this stage. It doesn’t tell you whether they can hear the sounds, whether they know the correct letters for those sounds, or whether working memory broke under the load of an irregular spelling.

But if you ask a child to spell ship and they write sip, you learned something clear. They heard /s/ and /i/ and /p/. They either didn’t hear /sh/ as a unit, or they haven’t stabilized the sh team as one sound, or they defaulted to a single letter because teams aren’t solid yet. Now you know what to teach: “In this word, the first sound is /sh/. We spell /sh/ with two letters working together: s-h. They are a team.”

That is a next step you can actually take.

Decodable words also protect working memory, which we’ve been honoring since Chapter 1. When a word can be built from sounds your child already knows, your child is not forced to hold multiple competing options in mind. They don’t have to decide between ai, ay, a_e, and eigh. They don’t have to remember that this word is “just weird.” The memory load stays low enough that your child can practice retrieval, and retrieval is what creates mapping.

This is especially important for the child whose page falls apart under pressure. When a child is overloaded, their spelling errors often look random, but they aren’t. They are the predictable output of a system that ran out of capacity. Decodable practice helps because it keeps the capacity demands within range. Then you can slowly widen the range, instead of throwing your child into deep water and calling it swimming lessons.

If dysgraphia is part of your child’s picture, decodable words also reduce the chance that handwriting will hijack the whole session.

A child whose hand is working hard does not have extra energy for complicated spelling choices. When spelling requires advanced pattern selection plus careful letter formation, the hand often wins and the

spelling collapses. But when the spelling is decodable, you can separate the jobs more cleanly.

You can do the mapping work with tiles first, where the child can focus on sound-to-letter bonds without the motor load. Then you can add one brief handwriting step, as we discussed in Chapter 5: “Now we write it one time.” One time is often enough to connect the mapping to the hand without turning the session into a battle.

Decodable words are also where “one sound at a time” becomes real.

Parents often try to help by picking a group of words that seem similar. Sometimes they are. Sometimes they only look similar on the surface. This is where many school lists get into trouble. A list might look like it’s teaching one thing, but it actually requires three or four different things.

Take a list labeled “long i.” It might include bike, light, night, pie, and fly. That’s not one pattern. That is multiple patterns for the same sound, plus silent e, plus vowel teams, plus sometimes a rule about y at the end. For a strong speller, it’s interesting. For a struggling speller, it’s overload disguised as a theme.

Decodable word work avoids that trap by controlling the difficulty. You pick words that mostly use one new pattern and otherwise rely on known code. That way, when your child succeeds, you can trust the success. It wasn’t luck. It wasn’t cramming. It was skill.

So how do you choose decodable words at home without becoming a curriculum designer?

You don’t have to be perfect. You only have to be more honest than the average spelling list.

Start by asking two questions:

What sounds and spellings does my child already know well enough to retrieve without heavy prompting?

What one new sound or spelling are we adding right now?

Then choose a small set of words that use mostly what is known plus that one new thing.

If your child is working on sh, you choose ship, shop, shut, cash, fish. If your child is working on ck as a spelling for /k/ at the end of short-vowel words, you choose back, neck, sick, rock, duck. If your child is working on

the idea that a word can end in -tch after a short vowel, you choose match, pitch, catch, not a list that also includes teacher and beautiful and because.

This kind of selection is not about keeping your child in a baby lane. It is about building automaticity in the base code so their brain has space for complexity later.

And yes, English has many words that aren’t fully decodable in the early stages. We are not pretending otherwise. But even “irregular” words often have parts that are regular. That’s why in Chapter 5 we modeled the correction for said: “You mapped the s and the d. This middle is the surprising part. It’s ai.” That is still decodable thinking. You are decoding the word into what follows the code and what has to be flagged.

But the building blocks are the words where nothing has to be flagged yet.

Here is one more reason decodable words matter for the emotional life of your home: they let you create real small wins.

In Chapter 4 we said a real win leaves the nervous system steady enough to store learning. Decodable words make that possible because they increase the odds that your child will experience success that is earned, not rescued.

A child who expects to fail begins to brace before they begin. A child who begins to succeed at a fair task starts to relax. And a relaxed brain maps better. This is not a motivational poster. This is the biology of attention and threat mode, playing out in your kitchen.

So if you are coming out of years of Friday lists, start here: give your child a short list of words they can actually build with what they know. Let them feel the clean click of sound to symbol. Let the practice be calm enough that your child can notice, “When I tap the sounds and choose the letters, it works.”

That moment is not small.

It is the beginning of a new story: spelling is not a talent you either have or don’t have. It is a system you build, one sound at a time, with words that behave honestly enough for the brain to store them.

In the next parts of this chapter, we’ll talk about sequencing more directly, how to choose the right next step, and how to pace instruction so you never have to overwhelm your child to make progress. But

decodable words are the first brick. They are where you stop asking your child to leap across gaps and start handing them a bridge they can actually walk on.

Sequencing is the part most families accidentally skip, not because they don’t care, but because no one ever explained that spelling instruction has an order that matters.

School spelling often feels like weather. A list blows in on Monday, you deal with it, and by Friday you either survived or you didn’t. The list might be “long a” one week and “silent letters” the next, and if your child struggles, the hidden message becomes, “English is random and you are bad at it.”

But for the learner we’re talking about, random is the enemy.

In Chapter 5 we translated Orton-Gillingham into family language: explicit, systematic, sequential, cumulative, diagnostic, and calm. This subchapter is where “sequential” becomes practical. Sequencing is simply choosing what to teach first, what to teach next, and what to delay until the foundation is strong enough to hold it.

Think of the relay race model from Chapter 2. If the baton keeps getting dropped, you don’t fix it by making the runner sprint faster. You fix the handoff. Sequencing is you deciding which handoff to train first, so your child can succeed often enough for orthographic mapping to actually happen.

Here is the truth that relieves a lot of guilt: if you teach things out of order, your child can look like they are “not learning,” when really they are being asked to build on air.

For example, many struggling spellers are pushed into multiple spellings for the same sound too early. They get a list that is supposed to teach “long i,” and it includes bike, light, night, fly, pie. That is not one lesson. That is several spelling choices for one sound, plus silent e, plus vowel teams, plus “sometimes y.” A strong speller can hold the options in mind and sort them. A struggling speller cannot. Working memory overloads, and then the child does what Chapter 1 warned us about: they guess, they cram, they pass Friday, and by Monday it’s gone.

Sequencing prevents that by controlling how many new decisions you ask your child to make at once.

A good sequence does three things:

It starts with the most reliable building blocks.

It adds one new piece at a time.

It reviews old pieces often enough that they become usable under load.

The phrase “under load” matters. Your child does not need to be able to spell a word only while staring at the model, sitting quietly, with you coaching every sound. They need to be able to retrieve spelling while also thinking, writing, and sometimes managing a hand that won’t cooperate. That is why Chapter 3 and Chapter 4 belong in the same book as Chapter 6. Sequencing is not only about the code. It is about the total load on your child’s system.

So what comes first?

For most struggling learners, the first lane is simple, consistent sound-to- letter mapping with decodable words, exactly where 6.1 ended. Short vowels. Common consonants. High-utility digraphs like sh, ch, th, when your child is ready. Clean words that “behave honestly,” as we said earlier.

This is not baby work. This is foundation work. It is where the brain gets enough successful repetitions of the mapping loop to start trusting it: say it, tap it, build or write it, read it, cover it, retrieve it, check and correct.

A practical way to think about sequencing is: before you ask your child to make spelling choices, make sure they can do spelling connections.

Connection comes first: “I hear /sh/ and I know sh.”

Choice comes later: “I hear /ay/ and I have to choose between a_e, ai, ay, eigh.”

Many school lists start with choice. Struggling learners need more time in connection.

How do you know when your child is ready to move forward?

Not by grade level, and not by how many worksheets you completed, but by stability.

Stability looks like this: when you say a decodable word your child has practiced, they can tap the sounds and build or write it with minimal prompting. They may not be fast. Speed is not the goal. They are accurate enough that the brain is rehearsing the correct form, not

rehearsing mistakes.

Stability also shows up in what happens when something changes. If you move from tiles to writing, does the spelling collapse? If yes, that tells you the mapping might be there, but the output load is still too high, which takes you right back to Chapter 3. You don’t have to abandon sequencing. You adjust the channel. You keep building the spelling system while you protect working memory from the hand’s demands.

Now, the second question families ask is the one that makes them feel unqualified: “What is the right order? I don’t know the scope and sequence.”

You do not need to memorize a full professional sequence to teach sequentially at home. You only need a few guiding rules that keep you out of the most common traps.

Rule one: Teach one new spelling feature at a time, and keep everything else familiar.

If you introduce sh this week, don’t also introduce a new vowel team and a new rule about endings. Choose words where sh is the only new thing.

That is why in Chapter 5 we used ship, shop, fish. Everything else is simple. One new piece gets repeated enough times to become stable.

Rule two: Do not teach multiple spellings for the same sound until one spelling is solid.

This is where many struggling spellers drown. English offers options. That is true. But the brain cannot hold a menu of options if the basic mapping is still shaky.

So you might teach the /k/ sound as c and k before you teach ck, and you might teach one common long-vowel pattern before adding alternatives. You’re not lying to your child about English. You’re sequencing English so your child can actually store it.

Later, when you add a second spelling for the same sound, you do it as a clear contrast, not a surprise. “We already know /sh/ is sh. Today we’re learning that /ch/ is ch.” Or, later: “We already know /ay/ can be a_e. Today we are adding ai, usually in the middle of a word.” One new option. Lots of practice. Then, and only then, another option.

Rule three: Teach high-frequency patterns early, not because they are simple, but because your child will meet them constantly.

This is where sequencing intersects with real life. Your child needs to spell words they actually use. That’s why we can’t live forever in “only decodable, only simple.” But we also can’t hand them the whole language at once.

So you aim for what gives the biggest payoff per unit of effort. Common consonant teams. Common endings. Patterns that appear everywhere. When you teach something your child sees daily, you give them more chances to retrieve it in the wild, which strengthens mapping beyond your fifteen minutes.

Rule four: Separate patterns from true irregulars, and handle irregulars with an explicit plan.

In Chapter 4 we lowered the temperature by changing the story from “You knew this” to “Not mapped yet.” Sequencing continues that honesty: some words will not be fully decodable when your child is still building the code. That’s not a personal failure. That’s English.

So you make two baskets in your mind.

Basket one is decodable practice words that match what you’re teaching.

Basket two is high-frequency irregular words your child needs for writing, words like said, was, have, what. These are not “memorize the whole shape and hope.” They are taught explicitly, with the parts that follow the code identified and the surprising part flagged, just like we modeled with said in Chapter 4 and Chapter 5.

Sequencing means you don’t mix these baskets randomly in the same lesson and call it one theme. You decide, “Today we do three decodable words for the pattern, and we do one irregular word as a special case.” That keeps the lesson coherent and keeps your child’s brain from feeling tricked.

Rule five: Let errors choose the next step.

This is the diagnostic principle from Chapter 5, applied to sequencing. If your child keeps omitting vowels, that is not a sign to move on. That is a sign to slow down and strengthen sound segmentation, because vowels are the glue in the middle of words and they are often the first thing to drop under working memory load.

If your child spells ship as sip, you don’t respond by adding more words and hoping repetition will fix it. You respond by teaching sh as a unit

more explicitly, then practicing it in multiple words while everything else stays constant.

If your child can spell accurately with tiles but not on paper, your next step might not be a new spelling pattern at all. Your next step might be controlling the output channel: fewer handwritten attempts, more spelling retrieval with tiles, and a separate, gentle handwriting lane, because the bottleneck is not the mapping alone. Chapter 3 taught you how real that bottleneck can be.

Now, here is what sequencing looks like in a real week at home, without making you become a full-time teacher.

You choose one focus for the week. One sound, one spelling team, one ending. Then you choose a tiny word set that uses it.

Day 1: Teach the new piece explicitly. “This sound is /sh/. We spell it with sh.” Practice with two or three decodable words, using the multisensory loop. End early with a win.

Day 2: Review the same words, then add one new word with the same feature. Retrieval is the goal. Cover and retrieve.

Day 3: Review again, then mix the words so your child can’t rely on order. This is how you build flexible retrieval, not just a memorized sequence.

Day 4: Do a calm check later in the day, like Chapter 4 suggested. “Can you build ship for me?” Not as a test, but as curiosity. If it’s shaky, that tells you to stay with the pattern.

Day 5: If the pattern is stable, you might add a tiny contrast, not a whole new topic. For example, if sh is stable, you might contrast it with s in simple words: sip versus ship. The contrast helps the brain notice what matters.

That is sequencing. It is not dramatic. It is not a special curriculum binder. It is you choosing an order that protects working memory and builds real mapping.

And there is one last sequencing principle that may be the most important for the child who has lived through shame: you move forward based on readiness, not on the calendar.

A typical system says, “New list every week, no matter what happened to your brain this week.”

Your system says, “We build until it sticks.”

That is how you climb out of the Friday-to-Monday loop. Not by trying harder on the same list, but by teaching in an order that makes forgetting less likely.

In the next section we’re going to talk about pacing, because even with a good sequence, you can still overwhelm a struggling learner by moving too fast, adding too many words, or expecting handwriting output to carry the whole lesson. Sequencing is choosing the right steps. Pacing is choosing the right speed. Both are what make “one sound at a time” not just a slogan, but a plan your child’s brain can actually use.

Sequencing chooses the right steps. Pacing chooses the right speed.

And for the child we are talking about, speed is not a motivational issue. It is a brain capacity issue.

Many families have already tried “more.” More words. More minutes. More worksheets. More copying. More pressure as Friday gets closer. If more worked, you would not be here. For a struggling learner, more can actually be the thing that breaks the system, because it quietly pushes the brain past the point where orthographic mapping can happen.

So this section is about a different goal: enough.

Enough practice to build a stable map, but not so much that working memory collapses, the hand gives out, or the nervous system flips into threat mode. Enough repetition to make it stick, but not so much repetition that your child starts associating spelling with punishment.

Pacing is how you keep practice in the learning zone.

The learning zone is that middle lane where the task is challenging enough to require attention, but not so hard that your child has to guess, melt down, or shut down. In Chapter 4 we called it lowering the temperature. In Chapter 3 we named the motor bottleneck that can steal the whole attention budget. Pacing is where those chapters become daily decisions.

Here is the simplest pacing truth you can hold on to:

When your child is overloaded, the brain does not store. It survives.

Survival can look like guessing. It can look like rushing to get it over with. It can look like “I don’t care.” It can look like tears. It can also look like the

quietest form of overload: your child complies, writes the words, and none of it sticks because they were operating on fumes.

If your child is dyslexic, overload often shows up as confusion at the sound level. The child stops being able to hear the sounds clearly, or stops being able to choose the correct grapheme even for things they knew yesterday. If your child is dysgraphic, overload often shows up as a visible breakdown in output: slower writing, cramped grip, increased erasing, degraded letter formation, missing letters, unfinished words. If your child has both, overload can look like everything falling apart at once, and you can feel the panic in the room.

That is not misbehavior. That is a system hitting its limit.

So what does good pacing look like at home?

It looks like smaller sets, shorter sessions, slower teaching, and more stopping.

Yes, more stopping. Because stopping at the right moment is not quitting. It is protecting the conditions that allow mapping to form.

Let’s start with the most common pacing mistake: adding too much new.

In 6.2 we talked about teaching one new feature at a time. Pacing says something even more specific: introduce that new feature in a tiny dose.

For a struggling learner, “new” is expensive. New requires extra attention. Extra attention uses working memory. When working memory is full, the child cannot hold the sound sequence steady long enough to retrieve and store the spelling.

So instead of teaching sh and then immediately practicing it in eight words, you might teach sh and practice it in two words. Two. Then stop.

Not because your child can’t do more in theory, but because you are playing the long game. You want the brain leaving the table with the feeling of “I did it” rather than “I barely made it.” That emotional ending is not just about self-esteem. It affects whether tomorrow’s practice begins in a calm state or a braced state.

Now the next pacing mistake: staying too long in one sitting.

Parents often assume that if a child is behind, the solution is to do longer sessions. But many struggling learners cannot hold high-quality attention for long stretches, especially after a full day of school. And dysgraphic

learners may literally run out of hand capacity.

If the practice starts calm and ends in tears, you have just taught your child that practice has no safe edge. The next time you say, “Let’s do spelling,” your child’s body remembers the ending. They brace earlier. They resist sooner. They melt down faster. What looks like “refusal” is often memory of overload.

This is why in Chapter 4 we said, “Stop one minute earlier than you think you should.” Pacing is that sentence, turned into a method.

A good home session often ends while the child could still do more.

That can feel counterintuitive if you grew up with the idea that you push through. But your child is not building muscle by pushing through fatigue. They are building a language-to-print mapping system. That system needs attention, accuracy, and safety. When those ingredients drop, continuing is not grit. It is noise.

So how long should a session be?

The honest answer is: short enough that it stays calm and accurate. For many children, that is ten to fifteen minutes. For some, it is eight. For some, it is five on a hard day. In Chapter 10 we will build a full fifteen- minute routine designed to survive real life, but you do not need to wait for Chapter 10 to start pacing well. You can start by making your time limit a form of kindness, not a form of control.

Try setting a timer and saying, “When it beeps, we stop. Even if it wasn’t perfect.” That “even if” matters. It tells your child that practice is not an endless trial.

Now let’s talk about pacing within the session, because overload can happen even in ten minutes if the pace is wrong.

Many children are pushed to go faster when the real need is to go slower.

But “go slower” cannot be a scolding. “Slow down!” often lands as “You’re doing it wrong again.” Instead, slow becomes a structure:

“We’re going to tap the sounds first.”

“We’re going to say each sound as we write it.”

“We’re going to cover and retrieve.”

“We’re going to fix it calmly if it’s wrong.”

That structure slows the process in a way that supports the brain, not in a way that shames the child.

It also gives you a way to spot overload early. Watch the quality of the tapping. Watch whether your child can still segment the sounds. Watch whether their writing speed suddenly collapses. Watch for the mid-word stall described in Chapter 3.3, the pencil hovering, the searching eyes, the tight grip. Those are early smoke signals.

When you see them, you do not push for “just one more.” You shift the demand.

That shift can be as simple as changing the output channel.

If handwriting is starting to steal the session, switch to tiles for the next word. Or let your child type. Or do the next two words orally, tapping the sounds and naming the letters without writing them, then write only one word at the end.

Remember: the goal of this chapter is one sound at a time, never overwhelming. The sound work is the core. Handwriting is important, but if dysgraphia is present, handwriting cannot be the gatekeeper that blocks spelling practice from happening at all.

You are allowed to protect the map by temporarily reducing the motor load.

Another pacing tool is something parents often forget they can use: fewer repetitions, but higher quality.

Many children are asked to write a word five times, ten times, fill a whole line. That can look like “practice,” but it often becomes copying, and copying is recognition, not retrieval. It also becomes motor fatigue for dysgraphic kids, which Chapter 3 warned you about. The hand gets tired, letter formation breaks down, errors increase, shame rises.

Instead, aim for two or three correct retrievals.

For example:

Look at the word.

Tap the sounds.

Write it once while saying the sounds.

Cover it.

Write it from memory.

Check it.

If it’s correct, do one more retrieval later in the session or later in the day. That is enough to start building stickiness without turning practice into a marathon.

If it’s incorrect, you correct immediately and calmly, as we described in Chapter 4.3, then try again. But you do not keep going until your child is exhausted. You keep going until you get one clean, correct retrieval, and then you stop.

That is pacing with respect for how mapping forms.

Now let’s address the question that can haunt parents: “But if we stop early, won’t we fall behind?”

That fear makes sense. But it rests on an assumption that time spent equals learning gained. For struggling spellers, time spent can equal stress gained, and stress often reduces learning gained.

The real measure is not minutes. The real measure is what your child can retrieve tomorrow without panic.

This is why the calm check from Chapter 4 is such a powerful pacing tool. It lets you measure whether today’s pace was effective without turning your home into a testing site.

If you practiced ship today, casually ask tomorrow, “Can you build ship for me?” If your child can retrieve it, your pace is working. If not, you didn’t fail. You simply learned that your child needs more repetitions across days, not more minutes in one sitting.

This is another key pacing truth:

For many struggling learners, shorter practice across more days beats longer practice in fewer days.

Mapping often consolidates between sessions, especially with sleep. Your job is to feed the brain small, accurate experiences often enough that consolidation can happen. You are not trying to force-storage through

intensity. You are building a habit of retrieval under safe conditions.

Now, because this is real life, we need a pacing plan for hard days.

Hard days happen. Your child didn’t sleep. School was brutal. The hand is tired. The nervous system is already hot. On those days, if you insist on the full plan, you may burn the whole week.

So you make a “minimum dose” plan.

Here is what it can look like:

Do one review word your child can succeed with.

Do one new word only if the child is still calm.

End.

Or even more minimal:

Do only oral tapping for two words while walking to the car or stirring dinner.

Or:

Do tiles, no handwriting at all.

This is not “skipping.” This is protecting consistency without triggering threat mode. Remember what we said in Chapter 5.2: calm and short is part of the method. The nervous system is part of instruction.

If you want a simple decision rule, try this:

If the session is increasing shame, stop.

You can say it plainly: “We’re not getting good learning right now. We’ll try tomorrow.” That sentence teaches self-regulation and preserves trust. It also models a truth your child needs to learn: capacity is real, and we can plan around it.

One more pacing piece matters, especially for older kids: let success set the tempo, not age expectations.

A ten-year-old practicing CVC words may look “behind” on paper, but if those sound-to-letter bonds are not stable, skipping them does not make the child more advanced. It makes them more fragile. The fastest way

forward is often going back to the last stable place and building from there with mastery.

That is not a punishment. It is the only route that keeps the foundation from collapsing.

And finally, pace your own correction.

When your child makes an error, you may feel urgency. You may want to explain more, correct more, fix everything. But long explanations can overload working memory just as surely as long lists do. Keep your correction brief and repeatable.

Tap.

Say.

Fix.

Try again.

That calm rhythm becomes the pace your child’s brain learns to trust.

If you take nothing else from this section, take this:

The goal is not to do as much as possible. The goal is to do the next right thing at a pace your child can store.

That is how “one sound at a time” becomes real. It is how you avoid overload without lowering standards. You are not giving up. You are choosing the only conditions under which orthographic mapping can actually happen for your child: small enough, slow enough, often enough, and calm enough.

In the next chapter, we’re going to build on this pacing protection by giving working memory a break through chunking: syllables, patterns, and the kind of grouping that lets a struggling learner hold more without feeling crushed by it. Because once pacing keeps the system from overheating, chunking helps the system handle bigger words without falling apart.

Chapter 7·Chunk It — Syllables and Patterns

that Give Working Memory a Break

Working memory is the mental notepad your child uses to hold information briefly while doing something with it. It is not long-term memory. It is not intelligence. It is not motivation. It is the small, temporary space where the brain keeps a few pieces of information available long enough to complete a task.

If you have a child whose spelling won’t stick, you have already seen working memory limits in action, even if no one ever used those words with you.

You say, “Spell fantastic,” and your child starts out fine, then freezes halfway through. Or they write fan and then stare at the paper like the rest of the word fell out of the sky. Or they can tell you the sounds out loud, but the moment the pencil touches the page, letters vanish. Or they begin with confidence, and by the end the word looks like a handful of correct letters in the wrong order.

That is not a character flaw. That is a working memory problem meeting a heavy load.

In Chapter 6 we talked about pacing, and we said something that can feel almost too simple: when your child is overloaded, the brain does not store. It survives. This is one of the main reasons. When working memory is full, there is no room left to do the careful, accurate attention that orthographic mapping requires.

It may help to picture working memory like a small tray your child is carrying across the kitchen. If the tray is empty, they can carry it easily. If the tray has three cups on it, they can still manage, slowly. But if you pile on twelve cups, a plate, a bowl of soup, and then ask them to open a door with their elbow, things are going to hit the floor. Not because they are careless. Because the tray has a limit.

Spelling and writing are some of the most tray-heavy tasks a child can be asked to do.

To spell a word during real writing, your child has to:

Think of what they want to say.

Hold the sentence in mind long enough to get it down.

Choose a word.

Break that word into sounds (for many dyslexic learners, this is already effortful).

Choose the letters or letter teams for those sounds (and if English offers more than one choice, hold the choices in mind).

Keep the letters in order.

At the same time, manage the output channel: form letters, control spacing, keep the pencil moving, or coordinate typing.

And while doing all of this, stay regulated enough emotionally that the brain is not in threat mode, which Chapter 4 taught us can shut down retrieval.

That is a lot of cups on a small tray.

This is why a child can “know” a spelling word in the controlled conditions of practice and then misspell it in a story, a science assignment, or a Monday morning journal entry. The knowledge may be there, or partly there. But working memory is carrying too much in the moment, and whatever is least stable drops first.

Often, what drops first is the middle of the word.

Parents notice this pattern all the time. Consonants may show up, but vowels disappear. Endings get chopped off. Letters flip. A child writes bgn for begin or frend for friend, or they produce a word that looks like a skeleton: the strongest sounds remain and the rest collapses.

This does not always mean the child “isn’t trying.” It often means the child’s working memory is protecting itself by simplifying. If the tray is overloaded, the brain will keep what feels most essential and let the rest go.

Working memory limits also explain why copying can look successful while spelling from memory falls apart.

Copying is not the same task. Copying lets the model carry part of the load. Your child can glance back and forth, using vision as an external storage system. That can make the tray feel lighter. But when you remove the model and ask for retrieval, the tray has to hold the whole sequence internally. If your child’s mapping is fragile, or if the motor output is demanding, the sequence can spill.

This is one reason we kept coming back to the difference between recognition and retrieval in Chapters 1, 4, 5, and 6. Recognition can happen with a full tray, because the word is right there. Retrieval is what collapses when the tray is overloaded.

Now add dyslexia and dysgraphia to this picture, because they change what gets loaded onto the tray.

A dyslexic learner may have to spend more working memory on hearing and segmenting sounds. The sound system is not automatic, so the child uses extra attention just to do what other children do without thinking: pull apart the word into phonemes and choose graphemes. That extra attention is not available for holding the sequence steady.

A dysgraphic learner may have to spend more working memory on moving the hand. The child may be thinking, “How do I make this letter? Is it facing the right way? Is it on the line? Why does my hand hurt?” The motor system is borrowing space from the tray. That is why a child can sometimes spell a word out loud correctly and then misspell it on paper. The spelling did not vanish. The tray got taken over by the hand.

And when both are present, the tray gets crowded fast: effortful sound work plus effortful motor work, all while trying to keep letter order stable. This is why our approach in this book keeps separating the jobs. We do not ask the weakest link to carry the entire task.

Working memory is also not consistent from day to day.

You have already lived this. Some days your child can do more. Some days the same task triggers collapse. That does not mean the child is manipulating you. It means capacity changes with sleep, stress, hunger, illness, sensory overload, classroom demands, and emotional temperature.

This is one reason the Friday-to-Monday loop is so brutal. Friday’s test might happen on a day when the tray is relatively empty and the child’s energy is up, and the child can hold the words long enough to perform. Monday’s writing assignment might happen after a rough weekend, a bad night, or simply a different emotional context, and suddenly the tray is full before spelling even begins.

So when you see inconsistency, don’t assume dishonesty. Assume load.

Here is a helpful way to think about the load in spelling. Some load is the word itself. Some load is the decision-making inside the word. And some

load is everything around the word.

The word itself: longer words require holding more pieces.

The decisions inside the word: English often gives multiple spellings for the same sound, which adds choice points. Every choice point is another cup on the tray.

Everything around the word: composing a sentence, remembering instructions, handwriting, being timed, feeling watched, worrying about being wrong, all of it takes space.

If you want to understand your child’s working memory limits more clearly, watch what happens when you remove one category of load.

Try this tiny experiment at home, not as a test, just as curiosity.

Take a word your child misspells in writing. Ask them to spell it in three different ways:

First, have them spell it out loud slowly while tapping sounds, with no writing.

Second, have them build it with tiles, still tapping sounds, with no handwriting.

Third, have them write it with a pencil.

If they can do it out loud but not with tiles, the mapping itself may still be unstable. If they can do it with tiles but not with handwriting, the motor channel is stealing the tray space. If they can do it in all three modes during practice but not in real writing, then “everything around the word” is the main load, and that tells you the next step is not more spelling lists. It is reducing composing pressure, using tools (Chapter 9 will go deeper), and teaching chunking so longer words stop feeling like one long tightrope.

Chunking is the promise of this chapter. Chunking means we stop asking working memory to carry the whole word as a single fragile string of letters. We teach the brain to group.

And this is important: chunking is not a trick. It is how working memory is designed to function.

Adults chunk constantly. You do not remember a phone number as ten separate digits. You remember it in groups. You do not remember a long

sentence as a chain of individual letters. You remember it in phrases. Grouping is how humans handle complexity without overload.

Struggling spellers often have not been taught how to chunk words in ways that help spelling. They have been told to “sound it out,” and when sound-it-out fails on longer words, the child concludes, “I can’t.” What they often really need is a way to reduce the number of items working memory has to hold at once.

Instead of holding seven or eight separate sounds, we might hold two syllables. Instead of holding a whole word, we might hold a base word plus an ending. Instead of holding a confusing letter choice, we might hold a known pattern as one unit.

That is what is coming next in this chapter: syllables and patterns that give working memory a break.

But before we go there, one more reassurance matters.

Working memory limits do not mean your child cannot become a strong speller.

They mean your child cannot become a strong speller through overload.

The path is not “try harder.” The path is “make the load smaller and smarter.”

That is why decodable words mattered in Chapter 6. That is why pacing mattered. That is why we lowered the temperature in Chapter 4. We have been protecting working memory all along, because working memory is one of the main gatekeepers of orthographic mapping. When the tray is not overloaded, the brain can actually do the careful binding work: sound to symbol to meaning, stored for later retrieval.

Now we widen the bridge.

We will keep our calm, explicit, multisensory loop. We will keep the focus on mastery over coverage. But we will start teaching your child how to carry bigger words without spilling them, by grouping the work into chunks the brain can actually hold.

That is not lowering expectations. It is finally matching expectations to how working memory truly works.

If working memory is a small tray, chunking is how you stop carrying every letter as a separate cup.

A lot of struggling spellers have been given one main tool: “Sound it out.” And sound it out is not wrong. It is the doorway into mapping. It is how we began in Chapter 6 with decodable words, one sound at a time, at a pace that stays calm. But as soon as words get longer, “sound it out” can become too many pieces to hold in order, especially when the child also has to manage handwriting, spacing, and the emotional pressure of getting it right.

Chunking keeps sound-based spelling, but it changes the size of what the brain has to hold at once.

Instead of holding twelve individual sounds in a long word, the child holds two or three chunks. Instead of a tightrope walk from first letter to last letter, the child gets stepping-stones.

The first kind of chunking we use is syllables.

A syllable is a beat in a word. It is often built around a vowel sound. If your child can learn to find the beats, they can stop treating a long word as one long, slippery object.

Take a word like fantastic. Many kids look at it and see a cliff. Their brain knows there are too many letters to hold steady, so they either rush, guess, or freeze halfway, just like we described in 7.1.

But fantastic has three clear beats: fan-tas-tic.

That does not magically make it easy, but it does make it doable. Three chunks are easier to carry than one long string. And something else happens when you syllabify: you give your child a place to pause and restart without losing the whole word.

This matters more than people realize. Many dyslexic and dysgraphic learners lose spelling not because they cannot begin, but because they cannot keep going. They start a word, and then the tray tips mid-word and everything spills. Syllables create safe “rest stops” in the middle.

How do you teach a child to break a word into syllables without making it a complicated lesson?

You start with the body. You already saw this logic in Chapters 5 and 6: we use the body to stabilize attention and mark units. The same is true here.

Pick one word you want to teach, not a whole list. Say it normally, then

say it slowly.

“Fantastic.”

Now clap it, tap it, or chin-drop it.

Clapping: fan (clap) tas (clap) tic (clap)

Tapping: tap the table once per beat.

Chin-drop: put a hand under the chin and feel how many times the chin drops when you say the word slowly. Fan-tas-tic has three drops. (This is not perfect for every accent and every word, but it is often helpful as a first tool.)

Choose the method that keeps your child calm. Remember the rule from Chapter 5.1: multisensory is only helpful when it anchors attention, not when it turns into chaos. For some kids, clapping is regulating. For others, it is overstimulating. You can be quiet about it. A small tap of the finger works just as well.

Once the child can feel the beats, you give them a simple instruction that changes everything:

“We’re going to spell one beat at a time.”

Not the whole word at once. One beat.

Here is what that can look like with the multisensory loop you already learned.

Say the first syllable: “fan.”

Tap the sounds in that syllable: /f/ /a/ /n/.

Write or build it: f-a-n.

Read it back: “fan.”

Then stop. Brief pause. Tiny win.

Now the next syllable: “tas.”

Tap: /t/ /a/ /s/.

Write: t-a-s.

Read it back: “tas.”

Then the next syllable: “tic.”

Tap: /t/ /i/ /k/.

Write: t-i-c.

Then read the whole word: fan-tas-tic.

If your child struggles, notice where. That is the diagnostic principle from Chapter 5.2, now applied inside a longer word. The mistake will often tell you whether the problem is hearing the syllable, hearing the sounds within it, choosing a grapheme, or holding the sequence through handwriting.

And if dysgraphia is part of the picture, this is where chunking can be a huge relief. Your child does not have to hold the whole word while the hand fights the pencil. They only have to hold one syllable at a time. You can also do the first pass with tiles, then write the word once at the end, or write syllable by syllable with a short pause between. You are controlling load on purpose.

Now, syllables are not just about clapping and dividing. They also carry patterns. This is where spelling starts to feel less like “remember this random sequence” and more like “I know what kind of syllable this is.”

You do not have to become a linguist to use this. You can start with one very practical syllable truth that helps many kids quickly:

Closed syllables tend to have a short vowel.

A closed syllable is one that ends in a consonant, like fan, tas, and tic. Those syllables usually use short vowels. When a child learns to see that, it reduces the number of vowel guesses they have to hold in working memory. It turns vowel choice from panic into pattern.

This is especially important because, as we said in 7.1, vowels are often the first thing to drop when the tray is full. Chunking by syllable gives vowels a job: each beat needs a vowel sound. If the child tries to skip it, you can gently point to the structure.

“Every beat needs a vowel. Let’s find the vowel sound in this beat.”

That sentence alone can change a child’s spelling behavior, because it

gives them a reliable check. Instead of writing a consonant skeleton and hoping, they begin to notice the missing piece.

Now let’s make this even more practical with a word your child might actually need: remember.

Many kids spell it like rember, or remebr, or they get lost halfway. The working memory tray spills in the middle.

Chunk it first by syllables: re-mem-ber.

Say it slowly: “re… mem… ber.”

Now you can do the same one-beat-at-a-time routine:

re (tap /r/ /ē/ or /r/ /ə/ depending on how your child says it, then spell it)

mem (tap /m/ /e/ /m/)

ber (tap /b/ /er/)

Notice something important: the goal is not to create a perfect linguistic analysis. The goal is to create a chunking system that makes the word writable. Different accents and pronunciations can change how a syllable sounds. That is okay. We are doing this in family language, not in a phonetics lab. If the child can say it, tap it, and build it, you are winning.

Now, syllables are one kind of chunk. The other kind we use is pattern chunks inside a syllable.

This is where you treat certain letter teams and endings as single units, so working memory has fewer separate pieces to juggle.

You have already been doing this with digraphs like sh in Chapter 6. You taught the child, “sh is one sound.” That is chunking. Instead of s + h as two separate decisions, it becomes one chunk.

Now we expand that idea to other common chunks:

Common endings: -ing, -ed, -er, -est

Common blends and teams: st, nd, mp, nk, ch, th

Common spelling units: ck at the end of short-vowel words (back, neck, sick)

When your child learns to hold -ing as one chunk, a word like jumping stops being j-u-m-p-i-n-g as seven separate decisions and becomes jump + ing.

That is a working memory gift.

And it also builds toward the bridge we will cross in Chapter 8, where meaning becomes a memory hook. Because many chunks are not just sound chunks. They are meaning chunks. An ending like -ing carries meaning: happening now. A base word like jump carries meaning. When the child can attach meaning to chunks, the word has more hooks in the brain.

But we stay in the spelling lane for now. We are building a way to hold the word steady.

Here is a simple home routine for chunking longer words without turning it into a lecture.

1. Say the word. 2. Ask: “How many beats?” 3. Mark the beats with claps or taps. 4. Spell one beat at a time using the tap-and-write loop. 5. Circle or underline any pattern chunk you want your child to start noticing (like sh, ck, ing). You do not need fancy markings. A quick circle is enough. 6. Read the whole word. 7. Do one retrieval attempt later: cover it, then write or build it again, beat by beat.

Notice what we did not do. We did not say, “Just sound it out” and walk away. We stayed with the child inside the word. We gave them a structure that reduces spill risk. And we kept the temperature low by treating mistakes as information.

If your child freezes mid-word, chunking also gives you a calm rescue that does not feel like rescuing.

Instead of, “Come on, you know this,” you can say, “You did the first chunk. Good. What’s the next beat?”

That one sentence takes the task from “spell the whole mountain” to “step to the next rock.” It also gives genuine credit for partial success, which matters for kids whose identity has been bruised by spelling.

Now, a crucial warning, especially for older kids: chunking is not babyish.

A ten-year-old who needs to clap syllables is not immature. They are using a brain tool. Adults do this too, just silently. The difference is that your child’s spelling system needs the chunking to be explicit for a while. Eventually, as maps strengthen, the chunking becomes internal. The child stops clapping, but the brain keeps grouping.

And one more important truth: chunking works best when you keep the words within reach.

If the word is wildly irregular or packed with multiple new patterns, chunking can still help, but it will not solve the entire problem by itself. Chunking is not a magic trick that makes any word easy. It is a load reducer. It creates space so that mapping can happen. But you still need the pacing and sequencing from Chapter 6: one new thing at a time, mastery over coverage.

So if you try chunking and your child still melts down, treat that as data, not failure.

Maybe the word is too complex for today.

Maybe the session is too long for tonight.

Maybe the hand is too tired and you need tiles or typing.

Maybe the nervous system is too hot and you need the minimum dose plan from 6.3.

Chunking is part of a system, not a standalone fix.

But when it clicks, you will see something that many parents find deeply relieving.

You will see your child spell a longer word without that mid-word collapse.

Not perfectly every time, not all at once, but with a new kind of steadiness. The tray is still small, but now the child is carrying fewer, smarter items. And that is the whole point of this chapter: we are not asking working memory to become bigger through sheer effort. We are teaching your child how to carry language in a way that fits the working memory they actually have.

Once your child can break a longer word into beats, the next relief comes from noticing that many beats are built from the same handful of patterns.

This is where spelling starts to shift from “hold this one word in your head” to “use what you already know.”

Patterns are the brain’s favorite kind of shortcut. A pattern lets working memory carry one chunk that represents many words, instead of carrying a brand-new sequence every time. And for the child whose tray is already crowded, that matters. It means fewer cups to balance. Fewer chances to spill mid-word. More stability when the word has to be spelled during real writing, with ideas and handwriting and emotion all happening at once.

But we need to be careful with the word “pattern,” because it can get misused.

A pattern is not a theme list that looks tidy on paper and still overloads your child. Remember the “long i” trap from Chapter 6, where one list secretly contains several different spellings and several different decisions. That’s not a pattern your child can use. That’s a menu of options served too early.

The kind of patterns that support memory are the ones that reduce decisions.

They answer questions like:

“When I hear this sound, what spelling do we usually use in this position?”

“When a short vowel is followed by this, what tends to happen?”

“What chunk shows up again and again at the end of words?”

“What do I already know inside this bigger word?”

Patterns make spelling feel less like a guessing contest and more like a system your child can lean on.

Start with the simplest, highest-payoff pattern idea: word families.

A word family is a group of words that share a chunk, usually at the end: -at, -in, -ack, -ump. In early spelling, these are a gift because they turn many words into one repeated structure.

If your child can spell back, they are very close to spelling sack, pack, tack, and black (once blends are stable). The new work is often only the first sound or blend. The chunk stays the same.

This matters because working memory doesn’t have to rebuild the whole word. It can hold the chunk -ack as one unit and swap the beginning. That is exactly the kind of “carry fewer, smarter items” we talked about at the end of 7.2.

Here is how you can make that visible in a calm, home-friendly way.

Write back.

Have your child tap and read it.

Then say, “We’re going to keep the ending chunk and change the first sound.”

Cover the word and build or write pack.

Then sack.

Then tack.

You’re not just practicing four words. You’re teaching the brain that chunks repeat, and that repetition is a memory hook.

If your child has dysgraphia and handwriting is the part that tips the tray, do this with tiles first. Tiles make the chunk literally reusable. Your child can keep -ack sitting there and swap the first tile. That physical action is multisensory chunking, and it is free working memory support. The chunk doesn’t have to be held in the head because it’s right on the table.

Then, if you want one handwriting connection, you can say, “Now write just one of them,” and let the child choose. Choice lowers threat, as we talked about in Chapter 4.3, and it also increases buy-in without turning the moment into a negotiation.

The next pattern type that supports memory is what we might call “spelling jobs,” especially at the ends of words.

English uses certain chunks because they do a job.

One of the most common is -ing.

When your child learns -ing as one chunk, you’re not asking them to spell i-n-g as three separate cups every time. You’re asking them to attach a known ending to a known base word.

Jumping becomes jump plus ing.

Sitting becomes sit plus ting? Not exactly, and this is where we stay honest and simple. Some base words change when you add endings. But the ending itself, the chunk -ing, stays constant and high-use. That constancy is what makes it valuable for a struggling learner.

You can teach this in a way that keeps the temperature low and keeps the lesson inside what your child can hold.

Write the base word: jump.

Read it.

Then say, “Now we add the ending chunk -ing.”

Write jumping, but visually mark the chunk boundary for your child by drawing a small line between the base and the ending: jump | ing. (Just a line. No fancy notation required.)

Then cover it and have your child rebuild it the same way: base first, ending second.

This is not yet the full meaning-based morphology approach we’ll build in Chapter 8, but it is already preparing your child for it. You’re showing that words are built, not just memorized. That message alone can reduce shame, because it tells your child there is a method, not a mystery.

Another pattern type is position-based patterns, the ones that reduce choice by telling your child “this spelling likes this spot.”

A classic example you may already have bumped into is ck at the end of short-vowel words: back, neck, sick, rock, duck. You don’t have to present this as a rule your child must recite. Present it as a helpful habit.

You can say, “In a lot of short-vowel words, /k/ at the end is spelled ck. That chunk helps the word look right.”

Then practice it in a small set, just like Chapter 6 taught you to do: one new thing at a time.

This kind of pattern reduces vowel drop, too, because it reinforces the structure “short vowel plus consonant ending.” It gives your child a reliable shape to expect in a beat.

Now let’s talk about vowel patterns, because vowels are often where

working memory collapses first. Many struggling spellers can hold consonants, but vowels feel slippery. The mistake adults often make is to respond by teaching more vowel options at once.

But the goal is not to teach all the options. The goal is to teach one option until it becomes usable.

Patterns support memory best when they are taught as “default for now,” not as “here are five choices.”

So you might start by reinforcing closed syllables with short vowels, like we briefly mentioned in 7.2. That alone reduces the panic of “which vowel do I pick?” because in many one-syllable words, the short vowel is the reasonable first try.

Then, when you eventually introduce a long-vowel pattern, you teach one, not all.

For example, you might choose silent e as the first long-vowel pattern because it is common and fairly consistent.

You teach it as a chunk: a_e is one pattern, not “an a and then later there’s an e that does something.”

You can literally draw it as a frame: a _ e.

Then you practice with a few decodable, controlled words where silent e is the only new thing: cape, tape, made, not a list that mixes in ai, ay, and eigh.

This is where pattern teaching has to stay married to pacing. A pattern is only supportive if your child can actually retrieve it without overload. If it becomes “another thing to remember,” it’s not a support. It’s another cup.

One more deeply supportive pattern is the kind that helps your child avoid letter-by-letter scrambling: consonant teams that act like one unit.

You’ve already done this with sh. Many kids also benefit from treating th, ch, and wh as one sound-chunk early on, because it reduces the chance of missing or reversing those letters under pressure. You’re telling the brain, “This is one piece.”

For older kids who still reverse or jumble letters, this is not babyish. It’s stabilizing. It reduces the number of separate items the tray must hold.

If your child spells with omissions like shp for ship, patterns can be the calm fix because you can point to the missing job.

You can say, “This word has three sound chunks: /sh/ /i/ /p/. The middle beat of this word needs a vowel. Let’s put the vowel in the middle.”

That’s pattern support. Not “be careful,” but “words have structure.”

Now let’s connect patterns to the moment where many kids freeze: the mid-word blank.

When your child gets stuck halfway through a longer word, it can feel like the rest vanished. Often, what vanished is not the whole word, but the next chunk.

Patterns give you a better rescue than supplying the letters, because they help your child re-enter the word without shame.

Instead of, “Sound it out again from the start,” you can say, “What chunk comes next?”

Or, “What’s the next beat?”

Or, “Do you hear an ending chunk like -ing or -ed?”

If the child can identify the chunk, you have turned a blank into a next step.

And that matters emotionally. A child who is used to collapse needs experiences of recovery. Not rescue, not being told the answer, but being guided back to a strategy that works. That’s how you build both spelling skill and resilience without turning practice into a test of courage.

A practical way to strengthen pattern memory without overheating your child is to build what we might call a tiny pattern bank.

Not a huge chart on the wall. Not a binder. Just a short list of patterns you are currently using, written on a single sheet or index card, like:

sh says /sh/ ck at the end after a short vowel -ing ending chunk

Then, during practice, you refer to it like a tool, not like a crutch.

“Let’s check our pattern bank. What do we use for /sh/?”

This is still retrieval practice, but it is supported retrieval. The goal is not to catch your child without help. The goal is to build the map with the right amount of support so the brain stores it.

Over time, you will use the pattern bank less, not because you force independence, but because the patterns become automatic.

That is the long game promise of this chapter: syllables and patterns let your child carry bigger words without spilling them.

And as you use patterns, something else begins to happen that sets us up for the next chapter.

Your child starts to notice that spelling is not only about letters. It’s about building blocks. Some building blocks are sound-based. Some are position- based. And some, eventually, are meaning-based.

When meaning gets involved, memory gets an even stronger hook.

That’s where we’re headed next: how meaning, through morphology, can make spelling stick in a way that pure repetition often can’t. But for now, patterns are your working memory ally. They let your child stop carrying every word alone and start carrying a system.

Chapter 8·Meaning as the Memory Hook —

Morphology and Making Spelling Stick

By the end of Chapter 7, your child has two kinds of relief: they can carry fewer pieces at once, and they have ways to recover when the middle of the word starts to wobble. Syllables give rest stops. Patterns give shortcuts. And for many learners, that alone changes the experience from “I can’t” to “I can try.”

But there is a third kind of relief that often becomes the turning point, especially for older struggling spellers and for children who have been stuck in the Friday-to-Monday loop for years.

That relief is meaning.

Meaning is the memory hook that makes a spelling feel less like a fragile sequence of letters and more like a word with a job, a family, and a reason it looks the way it does.

This is where morphemes come in.

A morpheme is the smallest unit of meaning in a word. Sometimes a morpheme is a whole word by itself, like play. Sometimes it is a piece you attach, like re- (again) or -ed (past tense). Sometimes it is a root that shows up inside a bunch of words, like struct in construct, structure, and instruct.

Here is why that matters for spelling.

When a child is relying only on sound, spelling works best in the clean, decodable lane we built in Chapter 6. Sound-to-letter mapping is the foundation. We are not leaving it behind. But English spelling is not only a sound system. It is also a meaning system. And for many words, the spelling is holding meaning steady even when pronunciation shifts a little.

That can feel unfair until you see it. Then it starts to feel like one of the few parts of English that is actually dependable.

Think about the word jumped from Chapter 7.3. When you add -ed to a base word, the ending can sound different depending on the word. Jumped ends with a /t/ sound. Played ends with a /d/ sound. Wanted ends with an /id/ sound. If your child tries to spell only by sound, they might be tempted to write jumpt or playd or wantid.

And if you only tell them “No, it’s wrong,” you have not given them a

system. You have given them another mystery.

Morphology gives the system: -ed means “already happened,” and we spell it -ed even when it sounds like /t/ or /d/.

In other words, the spelling is loyal to meaning.

This is a huge shift for a child who has been told, over and over, “Just sound it out,” and has learned the painful truth that sounding it out sometimes gets you close but not correct.

Morphology does not replace phonics. It supports it by giving the brain another anchor. Remember the line from Chapter 5.1: multisensory instruction gives the brain more than one path back to the answer. Morphology is one of those paths. It adds a meaning-path that can stabilize spelling when sound alone is not enough.

And it is especially helpful when working memory is crowded.

When your child is writing a sentence, their working memory tray is carrying ideas, word order, handwriting, punctuation, and maybe some emotional bracing. If the spelling of a word requires them to juggle multiple sound-based choices, the tray tips faster. But if the word can be built from a known base plus a known meaning-chunk, the number of decisions drops.

Instead of carrying “How do I spell that whole word?” they carry “Base word plus ending.”

That is fewer, smarter items. That is Chapter 7, but with meaning.

Let’s start with the most practical morphemes for families: prefixes and suffixes.

Prefixes go at the beginning. Suffixes go at the end. Both carry meaning.

Re- means again.

Un- means not.

Pre- means before.

Mis- means wrong or badly.

And common suffixes include:

-s or -es means more than one.

-ed means already happened.

-ing means happening now.

-er can mean “one who does” (teacher) or “more” (faster).

-est can mean “most” (fastest).

-ful means full of (helpful).

-less means without (hopeless).

When you teach these, you are not just teaching spelling. You are giving your child handles.

A handle is something the brain can grab.

Many struggling spellers have been asked to hold whole words as slippery objects. Morphemes give the word edges.

Now, a quick reassurance, because this can sound like we are about to move into advanced vocabulary lessons. You do not need to turn your kitchen table into a linguistics seminar. You also do not need to wait until your child is “ready for morphology” by grade level. If your child is writing words with -s, -ed, or -ing, they are already using morphemes. We are simply making the meaning units visible so they can become memory hooks.

Here is what it looks like in a calm, home-sized lesson.

Pick one base word your child can spell decodably, like jump.

You write jump. Your child reads it.

Then you say, “This is the base word. It carries the main meaning. Now we’re going to add a meaning chunk that tells when it happened.”

You add -ed: jumped.

And you physically mark the boundary with a small line, just like you did in Chapter 7 when chunking endings: jump | ed.

You can say, “Even though we say /t/ at the end, we spell the past tense chunk as ed. That’s the spelling for that meaning.”

Now cover it and retrieve it, the way we have been practicing all along. Not a ten-word list. One word built three times, correctly, calmly.

Look, cover, write, check.

And because we are keeping the Orton-Gillingham principles from Chapter 5, you also review. The next day, you briefly build jump, jumped, jumping. Same base, different meaning chunks. That repetition across days helps the map consolidate without overheating your child.

Notice what we just did: we reduced spelling load and increased understanding at the same time.

That understanding matters emotionally, too. A child who feels like English is a bag of tricks begins to relax when they see there are reasons. In Chapter 5.2 we said shame thrives in mystery. Morphology removes mystery.

Now let’s go one step deeper, because the biggest payoff of morphology is not only endings like -ed and -ing. The biggest payoff is that English often keeps the spelling of a base word consistent across a whole family of words, even when the pronunciation shifts.

This is where many older struggling spellers finally get a new explanation for a pattern they have noticed but couldn’t name.

Think about sign and signal.

In sign, the g is silent. Many children want to spell it sine, because that matches the sound.

But then we have signal, where the g shows up clearly.

The spelling sign is holding onto the base that carries meaning, the idea of a mark or indication. The g is not there to annoy your child. It is there because sign belongs to a word family: sign, signal, signature, design.

This is one of those moments where a child’s brain can stop thinking, “Spelling is random,” and start thinking, “Spelling is connected.”

Connected is what memory likes.

Here is the important teaching move: you do not start by saying, “The g is silent, just remember it.” That drops you back into the memorization myth from Chapter 1.

Instead you say, “This word is part of a family. The g shows up in other family words.”

Then you show it.

Write sign.

Write signal.

Circle the shared chunk sign in both words.

You can say, “This chunk carries meaning. English often keeps the chunk spelled the same, even if one letter goes quiet in one word.”

This does not require your child to understand Latin. It requires them to notice that a word can have a family.

And once you start looking for families, you find them everywhere:

Heal, health.

Please, pleasant.

Magic, magician.

Enter, entrance.

Music, musician.

In each case, the spelling is staying loyal to the meaning-family more than it is staying loyal to the exact sound in one form.

That loyalty is a gift to the child who is strong in meaning and weaker in sound processing, which describes many dyslexic learners. Dyslexic kids are often bright thinkers with good reasoning and vocabulary. They can use meaning as a stable anchor even when phonemes feel slippery. Morphology lets you lean into that strength without abandoning the sound work that builds mapping.

And it helps dysgraphic learners too, for a different reason. If handwriting is stealing working memory space, the child needs fewer decisions while the hand is working. A consistent base word across multiple forms reduces decision points. Instead of “How do I spell that new long word?” it becomes “I know the base, and I add a chunk.”

Now, we need to say something carefully, because families sometimes overcorrect here.

Morphology is powerful, but it is not meant to be dumped on top of an unstable foundation.

If your child cannot yet reliably segment and spell simple decodable words, you do not abandon that and jump into roots and affixes as your main plan. Chapter 6 still stands: one sound at a time, decodable, sequential, never overwhelming. Morphology is a bridge, not a replacement. It becomes more helpful as your child has enough sound-to- letter mapping to spell the pieces.

So what does that look like in a balanced way?

It looks like adding one meaning-chunk at a time, attached to words your child can already spell or almost spell.

It looks like using morphology to explain “why” when a word is not behaving purely by sound.
